Interpreting 'كل ج ب': Objection and Refutation
Objection and refutation regarding the meaning of the universal affirmative proposition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and evaluate the four possible interpretations of a universal affirmative proposition and explain why the objector's challenge fails.

Core Idea
The meaning of 'كل ج ب' cannot be identity, predication, coexistence, or an unknown fourth; each leads to logical failure.

This passage presents a sophisticated objection against the first mood of the first figure syllogism. The objector questions what exactly is meant by the universal affirmative proposition 'كل ج ب'. Four interpretations are considered and each is refuted. Understanding this dialectical exchange sharpens your ability to analyze propositional meaning in logical arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objector's challenge
The objector asks about the precise meaning of 'كل ج ب' in the first mood, suggesting that ambiguity undermines the syllogism.
2
List the four proposed interpretations
The four interpretations are: (1) identity of J and B, (2) J is described by B, (3) meanings of J and B coexist in one essence, (4) an unknown fourth meaning.
3
Examine the refutation of identity
The author uses the example 'كل إنسان حيوان' to show that human is not identical to animal; identity would also make the syllogism pointless and eliminate the necessary difference between terms.
4
Examine the refutation of predication
If J is described by B, then from 'minor is J' and 'J is B', it does not follow that minor is B, because the middle term's essence differs from the minor's. This breaks transitivity.
5
Examine the refutation of coexistence and the fourth
Coexistence fails because it makes the predicate the combination of J and B, contradicting the needed unity; the fourth is unknown and must be clarified by the objector.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم لقائل أن يقول هب أن الضروب المعتبرة هي هذه الأربعة لكن في كل واحد من هذه الأربعة من الشبهات ما فيه [1] منها أن يقال في الضرب الأول مثلا إذا قلنا كل (ج) ب)، فالمراد منه هو أن الجيم بعينه هو الباء، أو المراد منه هو أنه موصوف بصفة الباء، أو أن معنى الجيم ومعنى الباء يجتمعان في ذات واحدة، أو معنى هو رابع هذه الثلاثة. أما الأول فباطل بوجوه منها إنا إذا قلنا كل إنسان حيوان مثلا علمنا بالضرورة أن حقيقة الإنسان غير حقيقة الحيوان وكيف لا وقد كان الإنسان هو الحيوان مع الغير، ومن المحال أن يكون الحيوان مع الغير هو بعينه الحيوان لا مع الغير. ومنها أن الغرض من هذه الأقيسة هو الاستدلال على اتصاف الموضوع بالمحمول، واتصاف الشيء بنفسه غير معقول. ومنها أن الاختلاف في الماهية بين الحدود من اللوازم، ولو كان الموضوع عين المحمول فيما ذكرناه فلا اختلاف بين الحد الأصغر والأوسط البتة. وأما الثاني فباطل أيضًا فإن معنى القياس حينئذ هو أن الأصغر موصوف بالأوسط، والأوسط موصوف بالأكبر؛ ولا يلزم منه أن يكون الأصغر موصوفًا بالأكبر فإن حقيقة الأوسط مخالفة لحقيقة الأصغر. فلا يلزم من اتصاف الأوسط بشيء اتصاف الأصغر بذلك الشيء. نعم يلزم منه أن يكون الأصغر موصوفًا بالموصوف بالأكبر لكن لا يجب أن يكون الموصوف بالموصوف بالشيء موصوفًا بذلك الشيء. ولأن الاتحاد في الحد الأوسط من اللوازم، وإنه مما ينافي الاتحاد بالضرورة. وأما الثالث فباطل كذلك لأنه من جملة ما ينافي الاتحاد أيضًا؛ إذ المحمول هو الاجتماع بينهما حينئذ. وأما الرابع فذلك غير معلوم؛ فعلى المعترض بيانه إلى أن يعلم بأنه ما هو وكيف هو.
Then one may object: Even if the considered moods are these four, yet in each of these four there are doubts, among them [1] that it is said in the first mood, for example, when we say 'Every J is B', what is meant is that J itself is B, or that it is described by the attribute of B, or that the meaning of J and the meaning of B coexist in one essence, or a meaning that is a fourth to these three. As for the first, it is false in several ways, including that when we say 'Every human is an animal', we know necessarily that the reality of human is other than the reality of animal; how not, when human is animal with an additional factor, and it is impossible that animal with an additional factor is the same as animal without an additional factor. Also, the purpose of these syllogisms is to infer that the subject is described by the predicate, and a thing being described by itself is inconceivable. Also, difference in essence between the terms is necessary, and if the subject were identical to the predicate in what we mentioned, then there would be no difference between the minor and middle terms at all. As for the second, it is also false, because the meaning of the syllogism then is that the minor is described by the middle, and the middle is described by the major; and it does not follow that the minor is described by the major, for the reality of the middle is different from the reality of the minor. So it does not follow from the middle being described by something that the minor is described by that thing. Yes, it follows that the minor is described by what is described by the major, but it is not necessary that what is described by what is described by a thing is described by that thing. And because union in the middle term is necessary, and that necessarily contradicts union. As for the third, it is also false, because it is among things that contradict union as well, since the predicate then is the co-existence of the two. As for the fourth, that is unknown; so it is upon the objector to clarify it until it is known what it is and how it is.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes the first shubha (objection) against the first figure syllogism. The objector asks: in a universal affirmative proposition like 'كل ج ب', what exactly does it mean? Four possibilities are considered: (1) identity of J and B, (2) J is described by B (predication), (3) the meanings of J and B coexist in one essence, or (4) some unknown fourth meaning. Each is refuted. Identity fails because in 'كل إنسان حيوان' we know that human is not identical to animal; predication fails because if the middle term (J) is described by B, and the minor (say, D) is described by J, it does not follow that D is described by B, since the middle term's essence differs from the minor's; coexistence fails because it would make the predicate the combination of J and B, which also contradicts the needed unity; the fourth meaning is simply unknown and must be clarified by the objector. Thus the objector cannot undermine the syllogism by ambiguity.

Key Terms
كل ج ب
الموصوف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the first possible meaning of 'كل ج ب' considered in the objection?
A) J is described by B
B) J is identical to B
C) J and B coexist in one essence
D) An unknown relation

Key Takeaway
The universal affirmative proposition in a syllogism cannot be interpreted as identity, simple predication, or coexistence; understanding this refutation fortifies the foundations of categorical logic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the universal affirmative proposition 'كل مسكر حرام' (Every intoxicant is forbidden). Apply each of the four interpretations from the passage to this proposition and determine which one the author would accept as valid, based on the refutations given.
?
Construct a brief objection (shubha) against the first mood of the first figure syllogism using a different ambiguity, and then provide a refutation following the author's method.

The Requirement of Term Identity in the Categorical Syllogism
Objection about middle term identity in a categorical syllogism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the problem of term identity (ittihad al-hadd) in a categorical syllogism and explain why the middle term must be univocal.

Core Idea
A syllogism fails if the middle term is not identical in meaning across both premises.

In this passage, the author presents a subtle objection to a simple categorical syllogism: 'Every human is an animal, every animal is sentient'. The objection questions whether the term 'animal' in the minor premise is the same as the term 'animal' in the major premise. This probes a foundational requirement: the middle term must be truly identical, not merely homonymous. Understanding this requirement is critical for evaluating the validity of syllogisms in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the syllogism and its terms
The syllogism is: Every human is an animal; every animal is sentient; therefore, every human is sentient. Identify the middle term (animal), major term (sentient), minor term (human).
2
Locate the two interpretations of the predicate in the minor premise
The objection asks: is 'animal' taken absolutely (mutlaq) as a universal concept, or qualified (muqayyad) as individuals?
This is the core of the shubha.
3
Analyze the first horn: absolute interpretation
If 'animal' is absolute (the genus itself), then it is not the same as 'animal' in the major premise, which refers to individual animals. The middle term is not identical, and the syllogism fails.
The objection notes that 'animal qua animal' is different from 'every animal'.
4
Analyze the second horn: qualified interpretation
If 'animal' is qualified, we do not know the qualification; the expression is insufficient to guarantee identity. Thus, the syllogism does not produce necessary entailment.
The objection says the expression (lafz) falls short, so we cannot be sure of identity.
5
Conclude the logical lesson
The objection shows that for a syllogism to be valid, the middle term must be identical in meaning and reference in both premises; any ambiguity breaks the entailment.
This requirement is a necessary condition for validity in the categorical syllogism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٢] ومنها أي من الشبهات أن يقال إنا إذا قلنا كل إنسان حيوان، وكل حيوان حساس؛ فالمحمول في الصغرى لا يخلو من أن يكون هو الحيوان مطلقا، أو لا يكون بل يكون مقيدا. والأول باطل فإن المحمول في الصغرى على خلاف الموضوع في الكبرى حينئذ لما أن الحيوان من حيث هو الحيوان مغاير لكل واحد من الحيوان. وقد علمنا بأنه يصح أن يقال الحيوان جنس وعام ومشترك بين جميع الحيوانات، ولا يصح أن يقال كل حيوان جنس وعام أو مشترك أصلا. وأما الثاني فكذلك فإنه لا يدرى بأنه ما هو وكيف هو؛ واللفظ قاصر عنه فلا يدرى بأنه متحد مع الموضوع في الكبرى أم غير متحد. ولو كان كذلك فلا يلزم منه ومن أمثاله شيء؛ واللزوم من جملة الضروريات عند أهل المنطق.
[2] And among them (the objections) is that it is said: When we say 'Every human is an animal, and every animal is sentient', the predicate in the minor premise is either the absolute animal, or not, but rather qualified. The first is false, for then the predicate in the minor premise would be different from the subject in the major premise, since animal qua animal is different from every individual animal. And we know that it is correct to say 'animal is a genus and a universal and a common term among all animals', but it is not correct to say 'every animal is a genus', or 'a universal', or 'a common term' at all. As for the second, likewise, for it is not known what it is and how it is; the expression falls short of it, so it is not known whether it is identical with the subject in the major premise or not. And if that were the case, nothing follows from it and its likes; and entailment is among necessities according to the logicians.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage presents a second shubha regarding the categorical syllogism using the example 'Every human is an animal, every animal is sentient'. The objection questions the predicate in the minor premise: is 'animal' taken absolutely (مطلقا) or qualified (مقيدا). If absolute, then the predicate 'animal' in 'every human is an animal' is the genus itself, which is different from the subject 'animal' in the major premise 'every animal is sentient'? Actually, the argument says that if it is absolute, then the predicate in the minor premise (animal qua animal) is not the same as the subject 'animal' in the major premise, because animal qua animal is a universal concept, while the subject 'animal' in the major refers to individuals. It is valid to say 'animal is a genus' but not 'every animal is a genus'. So the syllogism fails because the terms are not unified. If the predicate is qualified (e.g., 'animal with a certain attribute'), then we do not know what that qualification is; the linguistic expression (اللفظ) is insufficient to indicate it. Thus we cannot know if the term is identical with the subject of the major premise. Without that identity, the syllogism does not produce necessary entailment, which is required by logicians.

Key Terms
المحمول في الصغرى
مطلق
مقيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the objection, if the predicate in the minor premise is taken absolutely (mutlaq), what problem arises?
A) The major premise becomes false.
B) The predicate differs from the subject in the major premise.
C) The minor premise becomes false.
D) The conclusion becomes known without proof.

Key Takeaway
The identity of the middle term across premises is a necessary condition for a valid categorical syllogism; any ambiguity breaks the entailment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the syllogism: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden; every nabidh is an intoxicant; therefore every nabidh is forbidden.' Apply the same shubha: is 'intoxicant' taken absolutely or qualified? Does the syllogism hold?
?
Find an example in fiqh where a qiyas fails because the 'illa is not truly identical between asl and far'. Explain how the failure mirrors the issue of term identity in this lesson.

The Third Shubha: Self-Inclusion of the Universal Major
Objection about universal major premise and self-inclusion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the self-inclusion paradox in universal categorical propositions and explain why the universal concept cannot be an instance of itself.

Core Idea
A universal term like 'animal' cannot include itself as an individual without contradiction.

When we make a universal claim like 'Every animal is sentient', we assume the term 'animal' covers all animals. But is the concept 'animal' itself an animal? The third shubha (objection) in classical logic shows that treating the universal as an instance of itself leads to a paralyzing paradox. This lesson unpacks that objection and its implications for syllogistic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Proposition and the Challenge
Locate the major premise under attack: 'Every animal is sentient'. The objector asks whether the universal concept 'animal' (al-ḥayawān min ḥaythu huwa al-ḥayawān) is included as an individual within the scope of 'every animal'.
This mirrors the self-predication problem in Platonic forms.
2
Set up the Dilemma
The objector presents two mutually exclusive and exhaustive options: either the universal 'animal' is included among the individuals of 'every animal', or it is not.
This is a true disjunction: it covers all logical possibilities.
3
Analyze Each Horn
If the universal is included, then it becomes an individual of itself, which is impossible because a universal (mushtarak) is shared by many and cannot be a specific instance. If it is excluded, then the universal concept falls outside the scope of 'every animal', contradicting the claim of universality (kulliyya).
Both outcomes are unacceptable, so the major premise is problematic.
4
Conclude the Effect on Syllogistic Validity
Since the major premise is flawed, the conclusion of the original syllogism ('Every human is sentient') does not follow. The objection shows that the syllogism is invalid unless the self-inclusion issue is resolved.
In later responses, logicians distinguish between the universal as a concept and its instances.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٣] ومنها أن يقال إذا قلنا وكل حيوان حساس: فالحيوان من حيث هو الحيوان لا يخلو من أن يكون داخلا في قولنا كل حيوان؛ وحينئذ يكون الحيوان من حيث هو الحيوان فردًا من أفراد الحيوان وذلك محال؛ إذ هو مشترك بين الكل والفرد أي فرد كان لا يمكن أن يكون مشتركًا. أو لا يكون داخلا؛ وحينئذ يكون الحيوان من حيث هو الحيوان خارجا عن مفهوم قولنا كل حيوان. وإنه مما ينافي الكلية؛ وقد كانت الكلية في الكبرى من اللوازم فلا يلزم إذن من هذا القول قولنا كل إنسان حساس.
[3] And among them is that it is said: When we say 'And every animal is sentient', the animal qua animal is either included in our statement 'every animal' or not. If included, then animal qua animal would be an individual among the individuals of animal, and that is impossible, for it is common (universal) to all and to any individual; it cannot be a common term (if it were an individual). Or it is not included; then animal qua animal is outside the concept of our statement 'every animal'. And that contradicts universality; and universality in the major premise is necessary, so from this statement it does not follow that 'every human is sentient'.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage presents a third shubha concerning the major premise 'كل حيوان حساس'. The objection asks whether the universal concept 'animal' (الحيوان من حيث هو الحيوان) is included as an individual within the universal quantifier 'every animal'. If it is included, then the universal concept itself becomes an individual of itself, which is impossible because a universal (مشترك) is shared by many and cannot be an individual instance. If it is not included, then the universal concept 'animal' falls outside the scope of 'every animal', which undermines the universality of the statement. Since the major premise claims universality (كلية), but the universal concept is excluded, the premise does not actually cover all animals, and therefore the conclusion 'كل إنسان حساس' does not follow. This objection attacks the coherence of using a universal term in a categorical proposition.

Key Terms
الكلية
مشترك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the consequence if the universal concept 'animal' is included as an individual in 'every animal'?
A) The major premise becomes false.
B) The universal concept becomes an individual, which is impossible.
C) The minor premise becomes false.
D) The conclusion follows immediately.

Key Takeaway
The self-inclusion paradox shows that universal terms in categorical propositions must be carefully defined to avoid treating the universal as an instance of itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Negation: Essence vs Title
Author clarifies how negation applies to meaning not just words, distinguishing essence and title.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between negation of essence and negation of title in a logical proposition, and explain why negation targets meaning rather than wording.

Core Idea
Negation targets meaning, not just the verbal expression.

In logic, negation is not always straightforward: when we say 'No J is A', are we negating the word 'A' from the word 'J', or the meaning of A from the meaning of J? This lesson explores a critical distinction between negation of the essence (salb al-dhāt) and negation of the title (salb al-ʿunwān), and why the logical purpose is always about meaning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of negation
The author introduces two ways negation can apply: negation of the essence (when the title and essence are synonymous) and negation of the title (when they are distinct). Read the first sentence and note the condition 'if the essence and the title are synonymous'.
Essence means the actual thing; title is the term used to refer to it.
2
Recognize the verbal conflation
A possible objection is raised: all negation appears to be of the title because we use words. The author acknowledges this verbally but counters that the purpose of language is to convey meaning.
Don't confuse the linguistic form with the logical intention.
3
Understand the author's response
The author argues that the real purpose of negation, as in 'Nothing is not from J to A' (meaning 'No J is A'), is to negate the larger concept (A) from the smaller concept (J), not the mere words. Focus on his statement: 'the purpose is negation of the larger term... not the negation of A insofar as it is the letter alif'.
4
Apply the distinction to an example
Take a concrete Islamic proposition: 'The world is not eternal.' Here, 'world' is J, 'eternal' is A. According to the author, the negation targets the meaning of eternity from the essence of the world, not just the word 'eternal'.
If the title 'eternal' and the essence of eternity were distinct, the negation could be of the title alone—but in this case they are identical.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عن الأصغر»، وذلك قد يكون بسلب الذات إذا كانت الذات والعنوان بمعنى واحد، وقد يكون بسلب العنوان إذا كان هذا بمعنى؛ وذلك بمعنى آخر. ولا يقال كيف هو والسلب في جميع الصور بسلب العنوان فإنه وإن كان كذلك فذلك بحسب اللفظ؛ واللفظ إنما يستعمل لتعريف معناه. فلو قلت لا شيء لا من (ج) أ) فالغرض منه سلب الأكبر وهو (أ) عن الأصغر وهو «ج» لا سلب «أ» من حيث هو لفظ الألف عن لفظ «ج» من حيث هو لفظ الجيم. وقد مرّ من قبل أن العنوان قد يكون عين الذات وقد لا يكون.
Concerning the minor term, that may be by negating the essence if the essence and the title are synonymous, or by negating the title if the title has one meaning and the essence another. And it is not said how is that, for negation in all forms is negation of the title; even if that is the case, it is with respect to the wording, and wording is only used to define its meaning. So if you say 'Nothing is not from J to A', the purpose is negation of the larger term, which is A, from the smaller term, which is J, not the negation of A insofar as it is the letter alif from J insofar as it is the letter jim. And it has passed before that the title may be identical to the essence or not.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage is a subtle clarification about the nature of negation in logical propositions. The author begins by discussing how negation can apply either to the essence (dhāt) or to the title (ʿunwān) of a term, depending on whether the title and the essence are synonymous or distinct. He then addresses a possible objection: one might say that negation is always of the title, not the essence, because we negate verbal expressions. The author responds that this is only verbally true; the purpose of the verbal negation is to convey a meaning—namely, to negate the larger concept (e.g., A) from the smaller concept (e.g., J). He illustrates with an example: when one says 'No J is A', the intent is not to negate the word 'A' from the word 'J', but to negate the meaning of A from the meaning of J. He concludes by reiterating that a title may or may not be identical to the essence, so the distinction matters.

Key Terms
سلب
ذات
عنوان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, in the proposition 'No J is A', what is primarily being negated?
A) The word 'A' from the word 'J'
B) The meaning of 'A' from the meaning of 'J'
C) The essence of 'J' from the essence of 'A'
D) The title 'J' from the title 'A'

Key Takeaway
Negation in logic targets the meaning of terms, not the verbal expression; the same verbal negation can have different logical import if title and essence differ.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Conditional vs Categorical Propositions
Comparison of conditional and categorical propositions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish conditional propositions from categorical propositions and identify their shared feature of being decisive statements.

Core Idea
Conditionals and categoricals both judge truth, but conditionals are compound relations, not simple subject-predicate.

This lesson compares conditional and categorical propositions. Both are decisive statements that can be true or false, but conditionals are necessarily composed of parts with a certain relation (following or opposition) rather than a simple attribution. Understanding this distinction is crucial for analyzing arguments in Islamic law and theology, where conditional rulings often appear.

Guided Path
1
Identify the common feature
Both conditional and categorical propositions are 'decisive statements capable of being affirmed or denied'—they are truth-apt.
2
Note the structural difference
Categoricals have a simple subject-predicate structure (e.g., 'Zayd is standing'), while conditionals are compound, comprising two parts (antecedent and consequent or disjuncts).
3
Recognize the judgment form
Despite being compound, conditionals still make a judgment relating part to part, but the relation is not one of simple predication or identity.
4
Observe the further division
Conditionals are then divided into conjunctive (mutasila) and disjunctive (munfasila) based on whether the relation is one of following (mutāba‘ah) or opposition (mu‘ānadah).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقضايا الشرطية توافق القضايا الحملية في أنها أقوال جازمة قابلة للتصديق والتكذيب، وتخالفها في أنها مركبة بالضرورة من أجزاء فيها تأليف جزئي. ومع ذلك فإن النسبة بينها ليست بنسبة أن يقال في الإيجاب إن أولها ثانيها كما يقال إن الإنسان عالم فيوافقها إذن في أن هناك حكما بنسبة جزء إلى جزء، ويخالفه في هيئة ذلك الحكم لكنها أي الشرطيات مختلفة أيضًا في هذه النسبة. فتكون النسبة الإيجابية في البعض منها على سبيل المتابعة، وفي البعض منها على سبيل المعاندة كما مرّ من قبل في بيان المتصلات والمنفصلات. وقد مرَّ أيضًا أن الاتصال إما تام وذلك فيما يكون اللزوم من الجانبين، وإما غير تام وذلك فيما يكون من جانب واحد. وكذلك العناد إما تام وذلك فيما يكون نقيض كل واحد منهما قائما مقام عين الآخر كما في قولنا هذا العدد إما زوج وإما فرد، وإما غير تام وذلك فيما لا يكون كذلك. ثم الكلام في الاتصال والانفصال مختلف في الكتب، [١] منهم من قال الاتصال مكان الإيجاب والانفصال مكان السلب؛ [٢] ومنهم من قال إن الشرطية من جملة ما لا إيجاب فيه ولا سلب؛ [٣] ومنهم من قال إن الشرطية المتّصلة إنما تكون شرطية بأن يكون مقدمها كالمشكوك فيه؛ [٤] ومنهم من قال إن قولنا كلَّما كان هذا إنسانًا فهو حيوان أو ما يجري مجراها فهو حملي لا شرطي لأنه بمنزلة قولنا كل إنسان حيوان. ولما كانت الأقوال مختلفة فيه كان من اللوازم أن ننظر أولا في الاتباع الذي في الاتصال فإنه قد يكون على أن وضع المقدم يقتضي لذاته أن يتبعه التالي؛ وهو بين في نفسه كما في قولنا إن كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود، فإن وضع الشمس طالعة يلزمه في الوجود وفي العقل أن يكون النهار موجودًا. وهذا المقدم ربَّما كان علة لوجود التالي كما في هذا المثال، وربَّما لم يكن كما مرّ من قبل، وربَّما كان وضع المقدم يلزمه التالي لا في العقل بل في الوجود حتى أن الوجود لا يخلو مع حصول المقدم عن أن يكون التالي معه لعلاقة بينهما فلا يمكن أن يحصل للمقدم وجود إلا وأن يحصل للتالي وجود وربَّما كان وضع المقدم لا يكون كذلك بل يكون المقدم إذا كان صادقًا كان التالي صادقًا من غير أن يكون بينهما علاقة كما في قولنا إن كان الإنسان موجودًا كان الفرس موجودًا لا على حكم منا أن ذلك الإنتاج أمر واجب في الوجود نفسه؛ ولا أن نفس وجود الإنسانية توجبه أو تمنعه بل على تجويز منا أن يكون اتَّفق اتفاقا وإن لم يكن الأمر في الطباع كذلك والقول الشرطي من حيث هو القول الشرطي يدخل فيه ما جميع ما أشرنا إليه.
Conditional propositions agree with categorical propositions in that they are decisive statements capable of being affirmed or denied, and differ in that they are necessarily composed of parts with a partial composition. Nevertheless, the ratio between them is not like the ratio that in affirmation one says 'the first is the second' as one says 'man is knowing'. So they agree in that there is a judgment relating a part to a part, and differ in the form of that judgment. But conditionals also differ in this ratio. So the affirmative ratio in some of them is by way of 'following' (al-mutāba‘ah), and in some by way of 'opposition' (al-mu‘ānadah), as previously mentioned in the explanation of conjunctives and disjunctives. It has also been mentioned that connection (al-ittiṣāl) is either complete, which is when implication is from both sides, or incomplete, which is when it is from one side. Likewise, opposition (al-‘inād) is either complete, which is when the negation of each one stands in place of the other itself, as in our saying 'this number is either even or odd', or incomplete, which is when it is not so. Then the discussion about conjunction and disjunction varies in the books: [1] Some said that conjunction takes the place of affirmation and disjunction takes the place of negation; [2] Some said that the conditional is among that which has no affirmation or negation; [3] Some said that the conjunctive conditional is only conditional if its antecedent is as if doubted; [4] Some said that our saying 'Whenever this is a human, it is an animal' or what runs in its course is categorical, not conditional, because it is equivalent to our saying 'Every human is an animal'. Since the statements differ on it, it was necessary that we first look into the following (al-ittibā‘) in conjunction, for it may be that the positing of the antecedent essentially requires the consequent to follow it; and that is clear in itself, as in our saying 'If the sun has risen, then day exists', for the positing of the sun having risen necessitates in existence and in the mind that day exists. And this antecedent may be a cause of the existence of the consequent, as in this example, or may not be, as previously mentioned. And it may be that the positing of the antecedent necessitates the consequent not in the mind but in existence, such that existence is not free when the antecedent occurs from the consequent being with it because of a relation between them, so it is impossible for the antecedent to have existence without the consequent having existence. And it may be that the positing of the antecedent is not like that, but rather the antecedent, when true, the consequent is true without there being any relation between them, as in our saying 'If man exists, then horse exists', not by our judgment that that entailment is necessary in existence itself, nor that the very existence of humanity necessitates it or prevents it, but by our allowance that they could coincide coincidentally, even if the matter is not so in nature. And the conditional statement, insofar as it is a conditional statement, includes all that we have indicated.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage classifies and analyzes the nature of conditional propositions. The author first compares conditional and categorical propositions: both are decisive statements accepting truth/falsity, but conditionals are necessarily compound. The relation between their parts is not a simple subject-predicate relation like 'man is knowing'. Instead, conditionals involve a ratio of 'following' (mutāba‘ah) in conjunctive conditionals, or 'opposition' (mu‘ānadah) in disjunctive conditionals. These come in complete and incomplete forms. Complete connection (ittiṣāl tāmm) means two-way implication; incomplete is one-way. Complete disjunction (‘inād tāmm) means the negation of each is equivalent to the other; incomplete is otherwise. The passage then lists four scholarly opinions on the nature of conditionals: (1) conjunction equals affirmation, disjunction equals negation; (2) conditionals have no affirmation/negation; (3) conjunctive conditionals are conditional only if the antecedent is doubted; (4) statements like 'Whenever this is human, it is animal' are actually categorical, not conditional. To resolve, the author examines the 'following' (ittibā‘) in conjunction. This can be of several types: (a) the antecedent essentially necessitates the consequent (e.g., sun's rising necessitates day's existence); (b) the antecedent is a cause of the consequent; (c) the antecedent necessitates the consequent in existence (not just in mind) due to a relation, so they always coexist; (d) the antecedent and consequent are merely coincident without any real relation (e.g., 'If man exists, then horse exists' is accidentally true). All these are included in the conditional statement. The author thus shows the range of logical connections in conditionals. For Islamic examples: 'If one has performed wudu, then prayer is valid' is a necessary connection due to divine law; 'If Zayd is in the house, then his brother is in the house' may be coincidental.

القضايا الحملية
أقوال جازمة
المتابعة
المعاندة

What do conditional and categorical propositions share according to the author?
A) They are both compound.
B) They are both decisive statements capable of being affirmed or denied.
C) They both have a simple subject-predicate relation.
D) They both involve opposition.

Key Takeaway
Both conditionals and categoricals are truth-apt judgments, but conditionals are compound with a relational ratio between parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the statement 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' (categorical) and 'If one drinks an intoxicant, punishment is due' (conditional), explain how they differ in logical structure and Islamic legal application.

Propositions

Types of Conditionals: Conjunctive and Disjunctive
Classification of conjunctive and disjunctive conditionals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify conjunctive and disjunctive conditionals and distinguish complete from incomplete forms.

Core Idea
Conditionals express either following (mutāba‘ah) or opposition (mu‘ānadah), each with complete and incomplete varieties.

This lesson classifies conditional propositions based on the relation between their parts. Conjunctives (mutasila) express a relation of following (ittibā‘): if the antecedent holds, the consequent holds. Disjunctives (munfasila) express opposition (ʻinād): the parts cannot be together or cannot both be absent. Each type can be complete (mutual implication or full exclusion) or incomplete (one-way). Understanding these distinctions is essential for correctly analyzing legal and theological arguments that use conditionals.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main types
Conjunctives (al-muttasila) use 'if...then' and express a relation of following. Disjunctives (al-munfasila) use 'either...or' and express opposition.
2
Determine completeness for conjunctives
Complete conjunction (ittiṣāl tāmm) occurs when implication is two-way (the antecedent implies the consequent and vice versa). Incomplete is one-way. Example of incomplete: 'If the sun has risen, then day exists' (sunrise implies day, but not necessarily reverse).
The passage does not give an explicit complete conjunctive example, but defines it as 'implication from both sides'.
3
Determine completeness for disjunctives
Complete disjunction (ʻinād tāmm) occurs when the negation of each disjunct is equivalent to the other disjunct. Example: 'This number is either even or odd' – if not even, then odd; if not odd, then even. Incomplete disjunction does not have that property.
4
Practice with Islamic examples
Classify the statement 'This act is either obligatory or prohibited' as incomplete disjunction because there are other categories (recommended, permissible, disliked).

Scholarly Opinions and Types of Following
Scholarly disagreements and analysis of following in conjunction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to summarize the four scholarly opinions about conditional propositions and distinguish the four types of logical following (ittibā') that the author presents.

Core Idea
Scholars debated the nature of conditionals; the author resolves by analyzing four types of following from essential implication to mere coincidence.

This lesson covers the four scholarly opinions listed in the passage regarding conditional propositions: (1) conjunction = affirmation, disjunction = negation; (2) conditionals have no affirmation/negation; (3) conditionals require a doubted antecedent; (4) some apparent conditionals are really categorical. The author then examines the concept of 'following' (ittibā') in conjunction to clarify the issue, distinguishing between essential, causal, existential, and accidental following. This analysis helps us understand the range of logical connections in conditional statements used in Islamic theology and law.

Guided Path
1
List the four scholarly opinions
Opinion 1: Conjunction takes place of affirmation, disjunction of negation. Opinion 2: Conditionals have no affirmation/negation. Opinion 3: Conjunctive conditionals are conditional only if antecedent is doubted. Opinion 4: Statements like 'Whenever this is human, it is animal' are categorical, not conditional.
These opinions show the diversity of views on how to treat conditionals logically.
2
Observe the author's method
Rather than siding with one opinion, the author says we must first examine 'following' (al-ittibā‘) in conjunction.
3
Identify the four types of following
Type A: Antecedent essentially necessitates consequent (e.g., sunrise → day). Type B: Antecedent is cause of consequent (same example). Type C: Antecedent necessitates consequent in existence (coexistence due to relation, e.g., always together). Type D: Mere coincidence (e.g., man exists → horse exists) without real relation.
Type D is not a necessary connection but accidental.
4
Understand the inclusiveness
The author concludes that all these types fall under the conditional statement 'as such' (min haythu huwa al-qawl al-sharṭī).

يكون النهار موجودًا. وهذا المقدم ربَّما كان علة لوجود التالي كما في هذا المثال، وربَّما لم يكن كما مرّ من قبل، وربَّما كان وضع المقدم يلزمه التالي لا في العقل بل في الوجود حتى أن الوجود لا يخلو مع حصول المقدم عن أن يكون التالي معه لعلاقة بينهما فلا يمكن أن يحصل للمقدم وجود إلا وأن يحصل للتالي وجود وربَّما كان وضع المقدم لا يكون كذلك بل يكون المقدم إذا كان صادقًا كان التالي صادقًا من غير أن يكون بينهما علاقة كما في قولنا إن كان الإنسان موجودًا كان الفرس موجودًا لا على حكم منا أن ذلك الإنتاج أمر واجب في الوجود نفسه؛ ولا أن نفس وجود الإنسانية توجبه أو تمنعه بل على تجويز منا أن يكون اتَّفق اتفاقا وإن لم يكن الأمر في الطباع كذلك والقول الشرطي من حيث هو القول الشرطي يدخل فيه ما جميع ما أشرنا إليه.
Conditional propositions agree with categorical propositions in that they are decisive statements capable of being affirmed or denied, and differ in that they are necessarily composed of parts with a partial composition. Nevertheless, the ratio between them is not like the ratio that in affirmation one says 'the first is the second' as one says 'man is knowing'. So they agree in that there is a judgment relating a part to a part, and differ in the form of that judgment. But conditionals also differ in this ratio. So the affirmative ratio in some of them is by way of 'following' (al-mutāba‘ah), and in some by way of 'opposition' (al-mu‘ānadah), as previously mentioned in the explanation of conjunctives and disjunctives. It has also been mentioned that connection (al-ittiṣāl) is either complete, which is when implication is from both sides, or incomplete, which is when it is from one side. Likewise, opposition (al-‘inād) is either complete, which is when the negation of each one stands in place of the other itself, as in our saying 'this number is either even or odd', or incomplete, which is when it is not so. Then the discussion about conjunction and disjunction varies in the books: [1] Some said that conjunction takes the place of affirmation and disjunction takes the place of negation; [2] Some said that the conditional is among that which has no affirmation or negation; [3] Some said that the conjunctive conditional is only conditional if its antecedent is as if doubted; [4] Some said that our saying 'Whenever this is a human, it is an animal' or what runs in its course is categorical, not conditional, because it is equivalent to our saying 'Every human is an animal'. Since the statements differ on it, it was necessary that we first look into the following (al-ittibā‘) in conjunction, for it may be that the positing of the antecedent essentially requires the consequent to follow it; and that is clear in itself, as in our saying 'If the sun has risen, then day exists', for the positing of the sun having risen necessitates in existence and in the mind that day exists. And this antecedent may be a cause of the existence of the consequent, as in this example, or may not be, as previously mentioned. And it may be that the positing of the antecedent necessitates the consequent not in the mind but in existence, such that existence is not free when the antecedent occurs from the consequent being with it because of a relation between them, so it is impossible for the antecedent to have existence without the consequent having existence. And it may be that the positing of the antecedent is not like that, but rather the antecedent, when true, the consequent is true without there being any relation between them, as in our saying 'If man exists, then horse exists', not by our judgment that that entailment is necessary in existence itself, nor that the very existence of humanity necessitates it or prevents it, but by our allowance that they could coincide coincidentally, even if the matter is not so in nature. And the conditional statement, insofar as it is a conditional statement, includes all that we have indicated.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage classifies and analyzes the nature of conditional propositions. The author first compares conditional and categorical propositions: both are decisive statements accepting truth/falsity, but conditionals are necessarily compound. The relation between their parts is not a simple subject-predicate relation like 'man is knowing'. Instead, conditionals involve a ratio of 'following' (mutāba‘ah) in conjunctive conditionals, or 'opposition' (mu‘ānadah) in disjunctive conditionals. These come in complete and incomplete forms. Complete connection (ittiṣāl tāmm) means two-way implication; incomplete is one-way. Complete disjunction (‘inād tāmm) means the negation of each is equivalent to the other; incomplete is otherwise. The passage then lists four scholarly opinions on the nature of conditionals: (1) conjunction equals affirmation, disjunction equals negation; (2) conditionals have no affirmation/negation; (3) conjunctive conditionals are conditional only if the antecedent is doubted; (4) statements like 'Whenever this is human, it is animal' are actually categorical, not conditional. To resolve, the author examines the 'following' (ittibā‘) in conjunction. This can be of several types: (a) the antecedent essentially necessitates the consequent (e.g., sun's rising necessitates day's existence); (b) the antecedent is a cause of the consequent; (c) the antecedent necessitates the consequent in existence (not just in mind) due to a relation, so they always coexist; (d) the antecedent and consequent are merely coincident without any real relation (e.g., 'If man exists, then horse exists' is accidentally true). All these are included in the conditional statement. The author thus shows the range of logical connections in conditionals. For Islamic examples: 'If one has performed wudu, then prayer is valid' is a necessary connection due to divine law; 'If Zayd is in the house, then his brother is in the house' may be coincidental.

The author resolves scholarly debates by analyzing the types of following in conjunction, ranging from essential necessity to accidental coincidence.

The Connected Conditional: Definition and Types of Implication
Author defines connected conditional and classifies types of luzum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the connected conditional and identify the different types of implication (luzum) that can hold between antecedent and consequent.

Core Idea
A connected conditional requires a relation of implication, which can be causal, constant conjunction, or even coincidental.

In Islamic logic, the connected conditional (shartiyya muttasila) is not simply an 'if...then' statement—its essence is the relation of necessary following (luzum). This lesson unpacks the definition and the various types of implication that the tradition recognizes, drawing directly from the author's discussion.

Guided Path
1
Extract the definition of the connected conditional
From the passage, identify the components: antecedent (muqaddam), consequent (tali), and the conditional particle. Note that the compound is truth-apt only as a whole.
The author emphasizes that the antecedent is not necessarily doubtful; it may be known false or true.
2
Identify the different types of implication (luzum)
The author describes three types: (1) causal implication (e.g., sun rising causes day), (2) existential conjunction without causation (constant concomitance), and (3) coincidental implication (e.g., 'if man exists, horse exists'). Locate these in the Arabic.
The conditional statement as such includes all these possibilities; the relation need not be real.
3
Recognize the essential condition
The author states: 'there must be a relation between them... otherwise the antecedent is not an antecedent, nor the consequent a consequent.' This relation can be real or stipulated.
Without any relation, the conditional is invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العناد إما تام وذلك فيما يكون نقيض كل واحد منهما قائما مقام عين الآخر كما في قولنا هذا العدد إما زوج وإما فرد، وإما غير تام وذلك فيما لا يكون كذلك. ثم الكلام في الاتصال والانفصال مختلف في الكتب، [١] منهم من قال الاتصال مكان الإيجاب والانفصال مكان السلب؛ [٢] ومنهم من قال إن الشرطية من جملة ما لا إيجاب فيه ولا سلب؛ [٣] ومنهم من قال إن الشرطية المتّصلة إنما تكون شرطية بأن يكون مقدمها كالمشكوك فيه؛ [٤] ومنهم من قال إن قولنا كلَّما كان هذا إنسانًا فهو حيوان أو ما يجري مجراها فهو حملي لا شرطي لأنه بمنزلة قولنا كل إنسان حيوان. ولما كانت الأقوال مختلفة فيه كان من اللوازم أن ننظر أولا في الاتباع الذي في الاتصال فإنه قد يكون على أن وضع المقدم يقتضي لذاته أن يتبعه التالي؛ وهو بين في نفسه كما في قولنا إن كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود، فإن وضع الشمس طالعة يلزمه في الوجود وفي العقل أن يكون النهار موجودًا. وهذا المقدم ربَّما كان علة لوجود التالي كما في هذا المثال، وربَّما لم يكن كما مرّ من قبل، وربَّما كان وضع المقدم يلزمه التالي لا في العقل بل في الوجود حتى أن الوجود لا يخلو مع حصول المقدم عن أن يكون التالي معه لعلاقة بينهما فلا يمكن أن يحصل للمقدم وجود إلا وأن يحصل للتالي وجود وربَّما كان وضع المقدم لا يكون كذلك بل يكون المقدم إذا كان صادقًا كان التالي صادقًا من غير أن يكون بينهما علاقة كما في قولنا إن كان الإنسان موجودًا كان الفرس موجودًا لا على حكم منا أن ذلك الإنتاج أمر واجب في الوجود نفسه؛ ولا أن نفس وجود الإنسانية توجبه أو تمنعه بل على تجويز منا أن يكون اتَّفق اتفاقا وإن لم يكن الأمر في الطباع كذلك والقول الشرطي من حيث هو القول الشرطي يدخل فيه ما جميع ما أشرنا إليه. ثم الشرطي المتصل إنما هو الشرطي متصل بحسب شرط وجزاء، والحقيقي منه هو الذي يكون إنتاج تأليفه لمقدمة على سبيل اللزوم عن وضعه. وقد مرَّ من قبل من الحروف ما يدل على الشرط، وإن المقدم فيه من حيث هو لا يكون قابلا للتصديق والتكذيب. وكذلك التالي فإن حرف الشرط مما يخرج كل واحد منهما عن كونه قضية حتى إذا قلت إن كان كذا لا صدق فيه ولا كذب / وإذا قلت فيكون كذا لا صدق فيها ولا كذب كذلك بل القابل للتصديق والتكذيب هو المجموع المركب من المقدم والتالي؛ وليس المقدم أيضًا من حيث هو مقدم للشك فيه أو للتصديق له بل إنما الالتفات إليه من حيث هو مقدم يلزمه التالي أو لا يلزمه فربَّما كان غير مشكوك في بطلانه كقولهم إن كانت العشرة فردًا فلا نصف لها بل ربَّما كان وضعه على أنه حق في نفسه ليصح به التالي. وأما من حيث هو شرطي فليس المقدم منه ولا التالي نقیضه فليس مشكوكًا فيه إذن. وأما بالنظر إلى أمر من خارج فربَّما صار التالي مشكوكًا فيه إذا كان القصد متجها نحو إنتاجه، أو المقدم إذا كان القصد متجها نحو إبطاله. وبالجملة فلا بد من علاقة بينهما ونسبة كيف كانت معلومة كانت تلك العلاقة أو غير معلومة وإلا لا يكون المقدم مقدما، ولا التالي تاليا. والعلاقة قد تكون بالحقيقة، وقد لا تكون بالوضع والتسليم، والمتصل على الإطلاق غير المتصل على التحقيق لا محالة. ولهذا ذهبوا إلى أن اللزوم في الحقيقي جزء من التالي؛ وليس جزءًا من التالي في المطلق فقولهم إن كانت الخمسة زوجًا فهو عدد حق من جهة؛ وليس حقًّا من جهة فإنه وإن لم يكن حقا في نفس الأمر فمن حيث إنه قياس حذفت منه مقدّمة كان حقا.
Contradiction is either complete, which is when the negation of each one stands in the place of the other itself, as in our saying 'this number is either even or odd'; or incomplete, which is when it is not like that. Then the discussion of conjunction and disjunction differs in the books: [1] Some said that conjunction takes the place of affirmation and disjunction takes the place of negation; [2] Some said that the conditional is among that which has no affirmation nor negation; [3] Some said that the connected conditional is only conditional by its antecedent being as if doubtful; [4] Some said that our saying 'whenever this is a human, it is an animal' or what runs in its course is categorical, not conditional, because it is like our saying 'every human is an animal'. Since the statements differed about it, it was necessary that we first look into the following that is in conjunction, for it may be that the position of the antecedent necessitates by itself that the consequent follow it; and this is clear in itself, as in our saying 'if the sun is rising, then the day exists', for the position of the sun rising necessitates in existence and in intellect that the day exist. This antecedent may be a cause for the existence of the consequent, as in this example, and it may not be, as previously mentioned; and it may be that the position of the antecedent necessitates the consequent not in intellect but in existence, such that existence is not empty with the occurrence of the antecedent of the existence of the consequent with it due to a relation between them, so it is not possible for the antecedent to obtain existence without the consequent obtaining existence; and it may be that the position of the antecedent is not like that, but rather the antecedent, if it is true, then the consequent is true without there being a relation between them, as in our saying 'if man exists, then horse exists', not on a judgment from us that that production is a necessary matter in existence itself, nor that the existence of humanity necessitates it or prevents it, but rather on a permission from us that it happened coincidentally, even if the matter is not so in nature. And the conditional statement, insofar as it is a conditional statement, includes all that we have indicated. Then the connected conditional is only a conditional connected according to a condition and a consequence, and the real (haqiqi) one is that whose composition produces a premise by way of implication (luzum) from its position. And it has previously passed among the particles that indicate the condition, and that the antecedent in it, insofar as it is, is not receptive to truth and falsity. And likewise the consequent, for the particle of condition removes each one from being a proposition, such that when you say 'if it is such', there is no truth or falsehood in it; and when you say 'then it is such', there is no truth or falsehood in it likewise. Rather, that which is receptive to truth and falsity is the composite whole consisting of the antecedent and the consequent. And the antecedent also is not, insofar as it is an antecedent, for doubt about it or for affirming it, but rather attention is paid to it insofar as it is an antecedent that the consequent necessarily follows or does not follow; so it may be not doubted in its falsehood, as their saying 'if ten is odd, then it has no half'; rather, it may be posited as true in itself to make the consequent true. And insofar as it is conditional, neither the antecedent nor the consequent is its contradictory, so it is not doubted then. And with regard to an external matter, perhaps the consequent becomes doubtful if the intention is directed towards its production, or the antecedent if the intention is directed towards its refutation. In summary, there must be a relation between them and a relation, however it may be, whether that relation is known or unknown, otherwise the antecedent is not an antecedent, nor the consequent a consequent. And the relation may be in reality, and it may be by stipulation and admission. And the conditional taken absolutely is not the same as the conditional in reality necessarily. And for this reason they went to the view that the implication (luzum) in the real (haqiqi) is part of the consequent, and it is not part of the consequent in the absolute. So their saying 'if five is even, then it is a number' is true from one aspect, and not true from another aspect; for even if it is not true in itself, from the aspect that it is a syllogism from which a premise was omitted, it is true.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage defines the connected conditional (al-shartiyya al-muttasila) and clarifies its essential conditions. The author begins by discussing contradiction (al-'inad) in general, distinguishing complete from incomplete contradiction, as a preamble to the discussion of disjunction. Then he notes that there are divergent opinions about the nature of conjunction and disjunction: some say conjunction corresponds to affirmation and disjunction to negation; some say conditionals have no affirmation or negation; some say the antecedent must be doubtful; some say universal conditionals like 'whenever this is a human, it is an animal' are actually categorical. Because of these differences, the author examines the notion of 'following' (ittiba') in the connected conditional. He explains that a connected conditional involves a relation of implication (luzum) between antecedent (muqaddam) and consequent (tali). This implication can be of several types: sometimes the antecedent is a cause of the consequent (e.g., sun rising causes day), sometimes it is not a cause but there is a constant conjunction, and sometimes there is no real relation, merely a coincidence (e.g., 'if man exists, horse exists'). Yet even in the latter, the conditional statement as such includes all these possibilities. The author then defines the real (haqiqi) connected conditional as one where the composition of antecedent and consequent yields a necessary implication ('ala sabili al-luzum) upon positing the antecedent. He emphasizes that the particle of condition (harf al-shart) renders the antecedent and consequent individually not subject to truth or falsity; only the compound proposition as a whole is truth-apt. The antecedent is not necessarily doubtful; it may be known to be false (like 'if ten is odd...') or even posited as true for the sake of the conditional. The essential requirement is that there be some relation between antecedent and consequent, whether known or unknown, real or merely stipulated. Without any relation, the conditional collapses. The author distinguishes between the absolute conditional (al-muttasil 'ala al-itlaq) and the real conditional (al-muttasil 'ala al-tahqiq). In the real conditional, the implication (luzum) is considered part of the consequent; in the absolute conditional, it is not. An example: 'if five is even, then it is a number' is not true in reality (since five is not even), but it can be considered true as an enthymeme (a syllogism missing a premise, because if we add 'every even number is a number', the conclusion follows). This nuanced explanation shows that the connected conditional is defined by the presence of a relation, not by the truth of its components.

The connected conditional is defined by the presence of a relation of implication (luzum) between antecedent and consequent, which can be causal, existential, or even coincidental.

Real vs Absolute Conditional and the Role of the Conditional Particle
Author distinguishes real vs absolute conditional and explains conditional particle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the real (haqiqi) and absolute (mutlaq) connected conditional and explain why the conditional particle renders the antecedent and consequent individually non-truth-apt.

Core Idea
In the real conditional, the implication is part of the consequent; in the absolute it is not. The conditional particle makes only the compound truth-apt.

The author distinguishes two notions of the connected conditional: the real (haqiqi) and the absolute (mutlaq). This lesson focuses on that distinction and the important role of the conditional particle (harf al-shart), which transforms the antecedent and consequent from standalone propositions into components of a single truth-bearing compound.

Guided Path
1
Identify the real conditional (haqiqi)
The author says: 'the real one is that whose composition produces a premise by way of implication (luzum) from its position.' Later he adds that 'the implication in the real is part of the consequent.' Find these statements in the Arabic.
In the real conditional, the relation of necessary following is built into the meaning of the consequent.
2
Contrast with the absolute conditional (mutlaq)
The absolute conditional is taken without the implication being part of the consequent. The author says 'the conditional taken absolutely is not the same as the conditional in reality necessarily.'
An example: 'if five is even, then it is a number' is not true in reality (since five is not even), but it can be considered true as an enthymeme.
3
Analyze the role of the conditional particle
The author explains that the particle of condition (harf al-shart) removes the antecedent and consequent from being truth-apt individually. Only the composite whole (majmu' murakkab) is subject to truth and falsity.
This is why 'if it is such' or 'then it is such' alone have no truth value.

كما في قولنا إن كان الإنسان موجودًا كان الفرس موجودًا لا على حكم منا أن ذلك الإنتاج أمر واجب في الوجود نفسه؛ ولا أن نفس وجود الإنسانية توجبه أو تمنعه بل على تجويز منا أن يكون اتَّفق اتفاقا وإن لم يكن الأمر في الطباع كذلك والقول الشرطي من حيث هو القول الشرطي يدخل فيه ما جميع ما أشرنا إليه. ثم الشرطي المتصل إنما هو الشرطي متصل بحسب شرط وجزاء، والحقيقي منه هو الذي يكون إنتاج تأليفه لمقدمة على سبيل اللزوم عن وضعه. وقد مرَّ من قبل من الحروف ما يدل على الشرط، وإن المقدم فيه من حيث هو لا يكون قابلا للتصديق والتكذيب. وكذلك التالي فإن حرف الشرط مما يخرج كل واحد منهما عن كونه قضية حتى إذا قلت إن كان كذا لا صدق فيه ولا كذب / وإذا قلت فيكون كذا لا صدق فيها ولا كذب كذلك بل القابل للتصديق والتكذيب هو المجموع المركب من المقدم والتالي؛ وليس المقدم أيضًا من حيث هو مقدم للشك فيه أو للتصديق له بل إنما الالتفات إليه من حيث هو مقدم يلزمه التالي أو لا يلزمه فربَّما كان غير مشكوك في بطلانه كقولهم إن كانت العشرة فردًا فلا نصف لها بل ربَّما كان وضعه على أنه حق في نفسه ليصح به التالي. وأما من حيث هو شرطي فليس المقدم منه ولا التالي نقیضه فليس مشكوكًا فيه إذن. وأما بالنظر إلى أمر من خارج فربَّما صار التالي مشكوكًا فيه إذا كان القصد متجها نحو إنتاجه، أو المقدم إذا كان القصد متجها نحو إبطاله. وبالجملة فلا بد من علاقة بينهما ونسبة كيف كانت معلومة كانت تلك العلاقة أو غير معلومة وإلا لا يكون المقدم مقدما، ولا التالي تاليا. والعلاقة قد تكون بالحقيقة، وقد لا تكون بالوضع والتسليم، والمتصل على الإطلاق غير المتصل على التحقيق لا محالة. ولهذا ذهبوا إلى أن اللزوم في الحقيقي جزء من التالي؛ وليس جزءًا من التالي في المطلق فقولهم إن كانت الخمسة زوجًا فهو عدد حق من جهة؛ وليس حقًّا من جهة فإنه وإن لم يكن حقا في نفس الأمر فمن حيث إنه قياس حذفت منه مقدّمة كان حقا.
Contradiction is either complete, which is when the negation of each one stands in the place of the other itself, as in our saying 'this number is either even or odd'; or incomplete, which is when it is not like that. Then the discussion of conjunction and disjunction differs in the books: [1] Some said that conjunction takes the place of affirmation and disjunction takes the place of negation; [2] Some said that the conditional is among that which has no affirmation nor negation; [3] Some said that the connected conditional is only conditional by its antecedent being as if doubtful; [4] Some said that our saying 'whenever this is a human, it is an animal' or what runs in its course is categorical, not conditional, because it is like our saying 'every human is an animal'. Since the statements differed about it, it was necessary that we first look into the following that is in conjunction, for it may be that the position of the antecedent necessitates by itself that the consequent follow it; and this is clear in itself, as in our saying 'if the sun is rising, then the day exists', for the position of the sun rising necessitates in existence and in intellect that the day exist. This antecedent may be a cause for the existence of the consequent, as in this example, and it may not be, as previously mentioned; and it may be that the position of the antecedent necessitates the consequent not in intellect but in existence, such that existence is not empty with the occurrence of the antecedent of the existence of the consequent with it due to a relation between them, so it is not possible for the antecedent to obtain existence without the consequent obtaining existence; and it may be that the position of the antecedent is not like that, but rather the antecedent, if it is true, then the consequent is true without there being a relation between them, as in our saying 'if man exists, then horse exists', not on a judgment from us that that production is a necessary matter in existence itself, nor that the existence of humanity necessitates it or prevents it, but rather on a permission from us that it happened coincidentally, even if the matter is not so in nature. And the conditional statement, insofar as it is a conditional statement, includes all that we have indicated. Then the connected conditional is only a conditional connected according to a condition and a consequence, and the real (haqiqi) one is that whose composition produces a premise by way of implication (luzum) from its position. And it has previously passed among the particles that indicate the condition, and that the antecedent in it, insofar as it is, is not receptive to truth and falsity. And likewise the consequent, for the particle of condition removes each one from being a proposition, such that when you say 'if it is such', there is no truth or falsehood in it; and when you say 'then it is such', there is no truth or falsehood in it likewise. Rather, that which is receptive to truth and falsity is the composite whole consisting of the antecedent and the consequent. And the antecedent also is not, insofar as it is an antecedent, for doubt about it or for affirming it, but rather attention is paid to it insofar as it is an antecedent that the consequent necessarily follows or does not follow; so it may be not doubted in its falsehood, as their saying 'if ten is odd, then it has no half'; rather, it may be posited as true in itself to make the consequent true. And insofar as it is conditional, neither the antecedent nor the consequent is its contradictory, so it is not doubted then. And with regard to an external matter, perhaps the consequent becomes doubtful if the intention is directed towards its production, or the antecedent if the intention is directed towards its refutation. In summary, there must be a relation between them and a relation, however it may be, whether that relation is known or unknown, otherwise the antecedent is not an antecedent, nor the consequent a consequent. And the relation may be in reality, and it may be by stipulation and admission. And the conditional taken absolutely is not the same as the conditional in reality necessarily. And for this reason they went to the view that the implication (luzum) in the real (haqiqi) is part of the consequent, and it is not part of the consequent in the absolute. So their saying 'if five is even, then it is a number' is true from one aspect, and not true from another aspect; for even if it is not true in itself, from the aspect that it is a syllogism from which a premise was omitted, it is true.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage defines the connected conditional (al-shartiyya al-muttasila) and clarifies its essential conditions. The author begins by discussing contradiction (al-'inad) in general, distinguishing complete from incomplete contradiction, as a preamble to the discussion of disjunction. Then he notes that there are divergent opinions about the nature of conjunction and disjunction: some say conjunction corresponds to affirmation and disjunction to negation; some say conditionals have no affirmation or negation; some say the antecedent must be doubtful; some say universal conditionals like 'whenever this is a human, it is an animal' are actually categorical. Because of these differences, the author examines the notion of 'following' (ittiba') in the connected conditional. He explains that a connected conditional involves a relation of implication (luzum) between antecedent (muqaddam) and consequent (tali). This implication can be of several types: sometimes the antecedent is a cause of the consequent (e.g., sun rising causes day), sometimes it is not a cause but there is a constant conjunction, and sometimes there is no real relation, merely a coincidence (e.g., 'if man exists, horse exists'). Yet even in the latter, the conditional statement as such includes all these possibilities. The author then defines the real (haqiqi) connected conditional as one where the composition of antecedent and consequent yields a necessary implication ('ala sabili al-luzum) upon positing the antecedent. He emphasizes that the particle of condition (harf al-shart) renders the antecedent and consequent individually not subject to truth or falsity; only the compound proposition as a whole is truth-apt. The antecedent is not necessarily doubtful; it may be known to be false (like 'if ten is odd...') or even posited as true for the sake of the conditional. The essential requirement is that there be some relation between antecedent and consequent, whether known or unknown, real or merely stipulated. Without any relation, the conditional collapses. The author distinguishes between the absolute conditional (al-muttasil 'ala al-itlaq) and the real conditional (al-muttasil 'ala al-tahqiq). In the real conditional, the implication (luzum) is considered part of the consequent; in the absolute conditional, it is not. An example: 'if five is even, then it is a number' is not true in reality (since five is not even), but it can be considered true as an enthymeme (a syllogism missing a premise, because if we add 'every even number is a number', the conclusion follows). This nuanced explanation shows that the connected conditional is defined by the presence of a relation, not by the truth of its components.

Structure and Truth-Aptness of Conditional Propositions
Defines conditional propositions and their truth-aptness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the antecedent and consequent in any conditional proposition and explain why only the whole compound is truth-apt.

Core Idea
Conditional propositions are truth-apt only as a whole, not in their parts.

In this passage, the author clarifies a fundamental feature of conditional propositions: neither the antecedent nor the consequent individually can be true or false. Only the composite of both carries truth value. This is crucial for understanding how conditionals function in logical reasoning and Islamic legal discourse.

Guided Path
1
Locate the conditional particle
Find the Arabic conditional particle (إن/إذا) that signals the start of the antecedent. In the passage, the author uses 'إن' in examples.
2
Identify antecedent and consequent
Separate the part after the particle (antecedent) from the following clause (consequent). For instance, in 'إن كانت العشرة فردًا فلا نصف لها', the antecedent is 'كانت العشرة فردًا' and the consequent is 'لا نصف لها'.
3
Check truth-aptness of parts
Notice that the author states that saying 'if such-and-such' alone carries no truth value. Confirm that neither the antecedent nor the consequent in isolation is a complete proposition capable of being true or false.
4
Identify the truth-bearer
The passage explicitly says 'the whole composite (المجموع المركب) of antecedent and consequent' is what can be true or false. This is a key conceptual shift from thinking of parts individually.

يكون التالي حقًا، فأما إذا كان حقا فلا يمكن أن يكون التالي باطلا؛ إذ المستلزم للباطل لا بد وأن يكون باطلا؛ والمستلزم الحق قد يكون حقا، وقد يكون باطلا كما مرَّ في قولهم إن الخمسة زوج. وعلى هذا إذا Then the conditional conjunctive is the conditional that is conjunctive with respect to a condition and a consequence, and the genuine one is that whose composition produces a premise on the way of implication from its setup. And it has already passed before from the particles what indicates the condition, and that the antecedent in it, inasmuch as it is, is not capable of being true or false. And likewise the consequent, for the conditional particle removes each one of them from being a proposition, such that when you say 'if such-and-such' there is no truth or falsehood in it, and when you say 'then such-and-such' there is no truth or falsehood in it likewise. Rather, what is capable of being true or false is the whole composite of the antecedent and consequent. And the antecedent also, inasmuch as it is an antecedent, is not for doubt about it or for affirming it, but rather attention is directed to it inasmuch as it is an antecedent from which the consequent follows or does not follow. Perhaps it is not doubted in its falsity, like their saying 'if ten were odd, then it would have no half'. Rather, perhaps its assumption is as true in itself to make the consequent valid. And as for from the perspective of being conditional, neither the antecedent nor the consequent is its contradictory, so it is not doubted then. And as for by consideration of something external, perhaps the consequent becomes doubted if the intention is directed towards producing it, or the antecedent if the intention is directed towards refuting it. In short, there must be a relation between them and a proportion, whether that relation is known or unknown; otherwise the antecedent would not be antecedent nor the consequent consequent. And the relation may be by reality, and may be by stipulation and acceptance. And the absolute conditional is necessarily different from the genuine conditional. For this reason they held that implication in the genuine conditional is part of the consequent, and it is not part of the consequent in the absolute conditional. So their saying 'if five were even, then it is a number' is true from one aspect and not true from another aspect, because even if it were not true in reality, yet from the aspect that it is a syllogism from which a premise was omitted, it was true. That is because the verifier of this proposition, when he assumes that five is even as true (and it was true in reality... but in itself, every even is a number), then the verifier at that time would be compelled that five is a number. The reason for it is the acceptance of a false matter along with the acceptance of a true matter. And it is not necessary to accept that falsehood upon one who accepts that truth. And the truth is that this acceptance is not valid with that acceptance, rather he must not accept this, for assuming five as even necessitates in reality that this not be accepted. And there is no harm in implying the impossible from the impossible. Then if the antecedent is false, it is not impossible that the consequent be true; but if it is true, it cannot be that the consequent is false, for what implies falsehood must itself be false, and what implies truth may be true and may be false, as passed in their saying 'if five is even'. And accordingly, when they say 'four is even', it is true in reality. But from the aspect that it is a syllogism from which a premise was omitted, it was false. That is because the verifier of this proposition, when he assumes that four is even as true, and it was true in itself that every even is a first (اولي), then the verifier at that time would be compelled that four is an even first. The reason for it is the acceptance of a true matter along with the acceptance of a false matter, contrary to that; for 'first' (اولي) is a term for a number which only one counts, and all evens are not like that. And it was said regarding such a statement, which is their saying 'four is even', that it is among the things that have discussion in it; because if someone claims that he knows that every four is even, he is asked: 'Is what is in your sleeve even or not?' If he says 'I have no knowledge of it', he is told: 'What is in your sleeve is four, and you do not know that it is even, yet you claimed that every four is even.' Then some people answered this by saying: 'We do not claim that every four is even absolutely, but we claim that every four that is among what we know is even.' And that is wrong, because we know that every four is in itself even, whether we know the whole or not. Rather, the answer regarding it is that we claim the knowledge that every four is in itself even, and this is knowledge by a universal judgment, and it does not follow from knowledge...
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition on the nature of conditional (shartiyya muttasila) propositions. The author begins by stating that a conditional conjunctive proposition consists of an antecedent (muqaddam) and a consequent (talin), connected by a conditional particle. The key point is that neither the antecedent nor the consequent in isolation is truth-apt; only the entire compound is capable of being true or false. The author illustrates this with examples: saying 'if such-and-such' alone carries no truth value, nor does 'then such-and-such'. The truth or falsehood attaches to the whole conditional. The function of the antecedent is not to be doubted or affirmed in itself, but to serve as a condition for the consequent. For instance, the statement 'if ten were odd, then it would have no half' uses a known false antecedent to show a logical consequence. The author then emphasizes that there must be a relation (nisba) between antecedent and consequent, whether real (bi'l-haqiqa) or by stipulation (bi'l-wad' wa'l-taslim). The genuine conditional (haqiqi) is contrasted with the absolute conditional (mutlaq): in the genuine, the implication (luzum) is part of the consequent, whereas in the absolute it is not. The discussion proceeds to analyze the logical behavior of conditionals. If the antecedent is false, the consequent can be true; but if the antecedent is true, the consequent cannot be false. This is demonstrated with the example 'if five is even, then it is a number' — considered from different angles: as a real conditional it may be false, but as an enthymeme (a syllogism with an omitted premise) it is valid. The verifier assumes the antecedent as true and adds the unstated premise 'every even is a number', yielding the consequent. The author then examines the case of 'four is even' and introduces a dialectical exchange regarding universal knowledge. Someone claims to know that every four is even; the objector asks about an unseen four, showing a gap between universal claim and particular knowledge. The author rejects a proposed answer that restricts knowledge to known instances, affirming that the knowledge claim is universal and does not require knowledge of every particular. This passage thus clarifies foundational issues in conditional propositions: their structure, truth conditions, and the nature of implication.

Genuine vs Absolute Conditional and the Role of Implication
Distinguishes genuine from absolute conditionals and clarifies the role of implication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish a genuine conditional (haqiqi) from an absolute conditional (mutlaq) and identify whether implication is part of the consequent.

Core Idea
In genuine conditionals, implication is part of the consequent; in absolute, it is not.

The author introduces two types of conditionals: genuine (haqiqi) and absolute (mutlaq). The key difference lies in the role of the implication (luzum). In genuine conditionals, the implication is considered part of the consequent, making the conditional stronger. In absolute conditionals, no such integration occurs. This distinction impacts how conditionals are evaluated in logical and legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the characteristic of genuine conditional
The passage says: 'they held that implication in the genuine conditional is part of the consequent.' Find this statement and use it as the defining feature.
2
Contrast with absolute conditional
The author notes that in absolute conditionals, implication is not part of the consequent. Recognize that this makes the absolute conditional 'the absolute conditional is necessarily different from the genuine conditional'.
3
Examine the example of 'five is even'
The passage uses 'if five were even, then it is a number' to illustrate. Note that as a genuine conditional it is problematic because the implication 'even implies number' is separate; as an absolute conditional it is acceptable because no implication is built in.
4
Apply the distinction to an Islamic example
Consider 'If the prayer is valid, then it removes impurity'. Determine whether implication is part of the consequent or not, and classify accordingly.
