ثم الشرطي المتصل إنما هو الشرطي متصل بحسب شرط وجزاء، والحقيقي منه هو الذي يكون إنتاج تأليفه لمقدمة على سبيل اللزوم عن وضعه. وقد مرَّ من قبل من الحروف ما يدل على الشرط، وإن المقدم فيه من حيث هو لا يكون قابلا للتصديق والتكذيب. وكذلك التالي فإن حرف الشرط مما يخرج كل واحد منهما عن كونه قضية حتى إذا قلت إن كان كذا لا صدق فيه ولا كذب وإذا قلت فيكون كذا لا صدق فيها ولا كذب كذلك بل القابل للتصديق والتكذيب هو المجموع المركب من المقدم والتالي؛ وليس المقدم أيضًا من حيث هو مقدم للشك فيه أو للتصديق له بل إنما الالتفات إليه من حيث هو مقدم يلزمه التالي أو لا يلزمه فربَّما كان غير مشكوك في بطلانه كقولهم إن كانت العشرة فردًا فلا نصف لها بل ربَّما كان وضعه على أنه حق في نفسه ليصح به التالي. وأما من حيث هو شرطي فليس المقدم منه ولا التالي نقیضه فليس مشكوكًا فيه إذن. وأما بالنظر إلى أمر من خارج فربَّما صار التالي مشكوكًا فيه إذا كان القصد متجها نحو إنتاجه، أو المقدم إذا كان القصد متجها نحو إبطاله. وبالجملة فلا بد من علاقة بينهما ونسبة كيف كانت معلومة كانت تلك العلاقة أو غير معلومة وإلا لا يكون المقدم مقدما، ولا التالي تاليا. والعلاقة قد تكون بالحقيقة، وقد لا تكون بالوضع والتسليم، والمتصل على الإطلاق غير المتصل على التحقيق لا محالة. ولهذا ذهبوا إلى أن اللزوم في الحقيقي جزء من التالي؛ وليس جزءًا من التالي في المطلق فقولهم إن كانت الخمسة زوجًا فهو عدد حق من جهة؛ وليس حقًّا من جهة فإنه وإن لم يكن حقا في نفس الأمر فمن حيث إنه قياس حذفت منه مقدّمة كان حقا. وذلك أن المحقق لهذه القضية إذا وضع أن الخمسة زوج على أنه حق وكان حقا في نفس الأمر بل في نفسه أن كل زوج عدد فيلزم ذلك المحقق حينئذ أن يكون الخمسة عددًا. والسبب فيه تسليم أمر باطل مع تسليم أمر حق؛ وليس يجب تسليم ذلك الباطل على من سلم ذلك الحق والحق أنه لا يصح هذا التسليم مع ذلك التسليم بل يجب أن لا يسلم هذا فإن وضع الخمسة زوجا يوجب في نفس الأمر أن لا يسلم هذا؛ ولا بأس من أن يلزم من المحال محال . ثم المقدم إذا كان باطلا فلا يمتنع أن يكون التالي حقًا، فأما إذا كان حقا فلا يمكن أن يكون التالي باطلا؛ إذ المستلزم للباطل لا بد وأن يكون باطلا؛ والمستلزم الحق قد يكون حقا، وقد يكون باطلا كما مرَّ في قولهم إن الخمسة زوج. وعلى هذا إذا قالوا إن الأربعة زوج فإنه حق في نفس الأمر. فأما من حيث إنه قياس حذفت عنه مقدّمة كان باطلاً. وذلك لأن المحقق لهذه القضية إذا وضع أن الأربعة زوج على أنه حق وكان حقا في نفسه أن كل زوج فهو أولي يلزم ذلك المحقق حينئذ أن يكون الأربعة زوجًا أوليا، والسبب فيه تسليم أمر حق مع تسليم أمر باطل على عكس ذلك؛ إذ الأولي عبارة عن عدد لا يعده إلا واحد؛ والأزواج كلها ليست كذلك. وقد قيل في مثل هذا القول وهو قولهم إن الأربعة زوج»، إنه من جملة ما فيه من الكلام؛ وذلك لأن واحدًا إذا ادعى أن له علما بأن كل أربعة زوج فيقال له هل الذي في كمي زوج أم لا؟ فإن قال لا علم لي به فيقال ما في كمي أربعة؛ ولم تعرف أنه زوج، وقد ادعيت أن كل أربعة زوج ثمَّ إن قوما أجابوا عن هذا فقالوا إنا لا ندعي أن كل أربعة زوج مطلقا بل ندعي أن كل أربعة هي من جملة ما عرفناه فهو زوج وذلك خطأ لأنــا نـعــرف أن كل أربعة فهي في نفسها زوج عرفنا الكل، أو لم نعرفه بل الجواب عنه أنا ندعي المعرفة بأن كل أربعة فهي في نفسها :زوج وهذه هي المعرفة بحكم كلي، ولا يلزم من المعرفة
Then the conditional conjunctive is the conditional that is conjunctive with respect to a condition and a consequence, and the genuine one is that whose composition produces a premise on the way of implication from its setup. And it has already passed before from the particles what indicates the condition, and that the antecedent in it, inasmuch as it is, is not capable of being true or false. And likewise the consequent, for the conditional particle removes each one of them from being a proposition, such that when you say 'if such-and-such' there is no truth or falsehood in it, and when you say 'then such-and-such' there is no truth or falsehood in it likewise. Rather, what is capable of being true or false is the whole composite of the antecedent and consequent. And the antecedent also, inasmuch as it is an antecedent, is not for doubt about it or for affirming it, but rather attention is directed to it inasmuch as it is an antecedent from which the consequent follows or does not follow. Perhaps it is not doubted in its falsity, like their saying 'if ten were odd, then it would have no half'. Rather, perhaps its assumption is as true in itself to make the consequent valid. And as for from the perspective of being conditional, neither the antecedent nor the consequent is its contradictory, so it is not doubted then. And as for by consideration of something external, perhaps the consequent becomes doubted if the intention is directed towards producing it, or the antecedent if the intention is directed towards refuting it. In short, there must be a relation between them and a proportion, whether that relation is known or unknown; otherwise the antecedent would not be antecedent nor the consequent consequent. And the relation may be by reality, and may be by stipulation and acceptance. And the absolute conditional is necessarily different from the genuine conditional. For this reason they held that implication in the genuine conditional is part of the consequent, and it is not part of the consequent in the absolute conditional. So their saying 'if five were even, then it is a number' is true from one aspect and not true from another aspect, because even if it were not true in reality, yet from the aspect that it is a syllogism from which a premise was omitted, it was true. That is because the verifier of this proposition, when he assumes that five is even as true (and it was true in reality... but in itself, every even is a number), then the verifier at that time would be compelled that five is a number. The reason for it is the acceptance of a false matter along with the acceptance of a true matter. And it is not necessary to accept that falsehood upon one who accepts that truth. And the truth is that this acceptance is not valid with that acceptance, rather he must not accept this, for assuming five as even necessitates in reality that this not be accepted. And there is no harm in implying the impossible from the impossible. Then if the antecedent is false, it is not impossible that the consequent be true; but if it is true, it cannot be that the consequent is false, for what implies falsehood must itself be false, and what implies truth may be true and may be false, as passed in their saying 'if five is even'. And accordingly, when they say 'four is even', it is true in reality. But from the aspect that it is a syllogism from which a premise was omitted, it was false. That is because the verifier of this proposition, when he assumes that four is even as true, and it was true in itself that every even is a first (اولي), then the verifier at that time would be compelled that four is an even first. The reason for it is the acceptance of a true matter along with the acceptance of a false matter, contrary to that; for 'first' (اولي) is a term for a number which only one counts, and all evens are not like that. And it was said regarding such a statement, which is their saying 'four is even', that it is among the things that have discussion in it; because if someone claims that he knows that every four is even, he is asked: 'Is what is in your sleeve even or not?' If he says 'I have no knowledge of it', he is told: 'What is in your sleeve is four, and you do not know that it is even, yet you claimed that every four is even.' Then some people answered this by saying: 'We do not claim that every four is even absolutely, but we claim that every four that is among what we know is even.' And that is wrong, because we know that every four is in itself even, whether we know the whole or not. Rather, the answer regarding it is that we claim the knowledge that every four is in itself even, and this is knowledge by a universal judgment, and it does not follow from knowledge...
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition on the nature of conditional (shartiyya muttasila) propositions. The author begins by stating that a conditional conjunctive proposition consists of an antecedent (muqaddam) and a consequent (talin), connected by a conditional particle. The key point is that neither the antecedent nor the consequent in isolation is truth-apt; only the entire compound is capable of being true or false. The author illustrates this with examples: saying 'if such-and-such' alone carries no truth value, nor does 'then such-and-such'. The truth or falsehood attaches to the whole conditional. The function of the antecedent is not to be doubted or affirmed in itself, but to serve as a condition for the consequent. For instance, the statement 'if ten were odd, then it would have no half' uses a known false antecedent to show a logical consequence. The author then emphasizes that there must be a relation (nisba) between antecedent and consequent, whether real (bi'l-haqiqa) or by stipulation (bi'l-wad' wa'l-taslim). The genuine conditional (haqiqi) is contrasted with the absolute conditional (mutlaq): in the genuine, the implication (luzum) is part of the consequent, whereas in the absolute it is not. The discussion proceeds to analyze the logical behavior of conditionals. If the antecedent is false, the consequent can be true; but if the antecedent is true, the consequent cannot be false. This is demonstrated with the example 'if five is even, then it is a number' — considered from different angles: as a real conditional it may be false, but as an enthymeme (a syllogism with an omitted premise) it is valid. The verifier assumes the antecedent as true and adds the unstated premise 'every even is a number', yielding the consequent. The author then examines the case of 'four is even' and introduces a dialectical exchange regarding universal knowledge. Someone claims to know that every four is even; the objector asks about an unseen four, showing a gap between universal claim and particular knowledge. The author rejects a proposed answer that restricts knowledge to known instances, affirming that the knowledge claim is universal and does not require knowledge of every particular. This passage thus clarifies foundational issues in conditional propositions: their structure, truth conditions, and the nature of implication.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what makes a conditional 'genuine' (haqiqi) as opposed to 'absolute' (mutlaq)?
A) The antecedent must be true
B) The implication is part of the consequent
C) The consequent must be true
D) The relation is always by reality

Key Takeaway
The genuine conditional integrates implication into the consequent; the absolute does not.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Find a conditional statement from a kalam text (e.g., al-Sanusiyya) and classify it as genuine or absolute, justifying your answer based on whether implication is part of the consequent.
?
Create your own genuine conditional on a fiqh topic and explain why the implication must be considered part of the consequent.

Propositions
intermediate
5 min
Truth Conditions and the Enthymeme Example
Explains truth conditions of conditionals and the enthymeme example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the truth of a conditional using the rule that a true antecedent necessitates a true consequent, and identify an enthymeme (omitted premise) in conditional reasoning.

Core Idea
A conditional can be valid as an enthymeme when an implicit premise supplies the connection.

The passage examines the truth conditions of conditionals, especially the rule that if the antecedent is true, the consequent must be true, but a false antecedent can have a true consequent. It then analyzes the example 'if five is even, then it is a number' as a syllogism with an omitted premise (enthymeme). This shows how conditionals can appear valid when a hidden premise is supplied. The author also discusses a dialectical exchange about universal knowledge regarding 'every four is even'.

Guided Path
1
State the truth condition rule
From the passage: 'if the antecedent is true, it cannot be that the consequent is false; if false, the consequent may be true.' Write this rule in your own words.
2
Analyze the 'five is even' example
The passage says this conditional is true from one aspect (as an enthymeme) and false from another (as a genuine conditional). Identify the hidden premise: 'every even is a number'.
3
Reconstruct the enthymeme as a full syllogism
Premise 1: Five is even (assumed true). Premise 2 (hidden): Every even is a number. Conclusion: Five is a number. Check validity.
4
Examine the dialectic on 'four is even'
The passage includes an exchange: someone claims 'every four is even'. An objector asks about an unseen four. The response and critique show the nature of universal claims. Identify the logical issue.

And it has already passed before from the particles what indicates the condition, and that the antecedent in it, inasmuch as it is, is not capable of being true or false. And likewise the consequent, for the conditional particle removes each one of them from being a proposition, such that when you say 'if such-and-such' there is no truth or falsehood in it, and when you say 'then such-and-such' there is no truth or falsehood in it likewise. Rather, what is capable of being true or false is the whole composite of the antecedent and consequent. And the antecedent also, inasmuch as it is an antecedent, is not for doubt about it or for affirming it, but rather attention is directed to it inasmuch as it is an antecedent from which the consequent follows or does not follow. Perhaps it is not doubted in its falsity, like their saying 'if ten were odd, then it would have no half'. Rather, perhaps its assumption is as true in itself to make the consequent valid. And as for from the perspective of being conditional, neither the antecedent nor the consequent is its contradictory, so it is not doubted then. And the relation may be by reality, and may be by stipulation and acceptance. And the absolute conditional is necessarily different from the genuine conditional. For this reason they held that implication in the genuine conditional is part of the consequent, and it is not part of the consequent in the absolute conditional. So their saying 'if five were even, then it is a number' is true from one aspect and not true from another aspect, because even if it were not true in reality, yet from the aspect that it is a syllogism from which a premise was omitted, it was true. That is because the verifier of this proposition, when he assumes that five is even as true (and it was true in reality... but in itself, every even is a number), then the verifier at that time would be compelled that five is a number. The reason for it is the acceptance of a false matter along with the acceptance of a true matter. And it is not necessary to accept that falsehood upon one who accepts that truth. And the truth is that this acceptance is not valid with that acceptance, rather he must not accept this, for assuming five as even necessitates in reality that this not be accepted. And there is no harm in implying the impossible from the impossible. Then if the antecedent is false, it is not impossible that the consequent be true; but if it is true, it cannot be that the consequent is false, for what implies falsehood must itself be false, and what implies truth may be true and may be false, as passed in their saying 'if five is even'. That is because the verifier of this proposition, when he assumes that four is even as true, and it was true in itself that every even is a first (اولي), then the verifier at that time would be compelled that four is an even first. And it was said regarding such a statement, which is their saying 'four is even', that it is among the things that have discussion in it; because if someone claims that he knows that every four is even, he is asked: 'Is what is in your sleeve even or not?' Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition on the nature of conditional (shartiyya muttasila) propositions. The author begins by stating that a conditional conjunctive proposition consists of an antecedent (muqaddam) and a consequent (talin), connected by a conditional particle. The key point is that neither the antecedent nor the consequent in isolation is truth-apt; only the entire compound is capable of being true or false. The function of the antecedent is not to be doubted or affirmed in itself, but to serve as a condition for the consequent. For instance, the statement 'if ten were odd, then it would have no half' uses a known false antecedent to show a logical consequence. The author then emphasizes that there must be a relation (nisba) between antecedent and consequent, whether real (bi'l-haqiqa) or by stipulation (bi'l-wad' wa'l-taslim). The genuine conditional (haqiqi) is contrasted with the absolute conditional (mutlaq): in the genuine, the implication (luzum) is part of the consequent, whereas in the absolute it is not. The discussion proceeds to analyze the logical behavior of conditionals. If the antecedent is false, the consequent can be true; but if the antecedent is true, the consequent cannot be false. This is demonstrated with the example 'if five is even, then it is a number' — considered from different angles: as a real conditional it may be false, but as an enthymeme (a syllogism with an omitted premise) it is valid. The verifier assumes the antecedent as true and adds the unstated premise 'every even is a number', yielding the consequent. The author then examines the case of 'four is even' and introduces a dialectical exchange regarding universal knowledge. Someone claims to know that every four is even; the objector asks about an unseen four, showing a gap between universal claim and particular knowledge. The author rejects a proposed answer that restricts knowledge to known instances, affirming that the knowledge claim is universal and does not require knowledge of every particular. This passage thus clarifies foundational issues in conditional propositions: their structure, truth conditions, and the nature of implication.

Why Coincidental Conditionals Are Useless for Science
Author categorizes coincidental conditionals into four truth-combinations and declares them useless for scientific inquiry.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a coincidental conditional (ittifāqiyya) and a necessitative conditional (luzūmiyya), and explain why only the latter is used in Islamic scientific discourse.

Core Idea
Coincidental conditionals are scientific dead ends: they yield no new knowledge.

The author of our text has just classified the possible truth-value combinations in coincidental conditionals. Now he delivers the decisive verdict: these conditionals are useless for scientific investigation. In this lesson, we will see why—and what distinguishes a worthless conditional from a valuable one in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Recall the definition of coincidental conditional
A coincidental conditional (ittifāqiyya) is one where the connection between antecedent and consequent is purely accidental, not due to any real entailment. For example, 'If man is speaking, then donkey is braying' has no real link.
2
Examine the four truth-combinations
The author shows that in such conditionals, the two premises can be both true, both false, or one true and one false. This is not possible in a necessitative conditional where the truth-values must align.
3
Understand the epistemological problem
The author argues that in a coincidental conditional syllogism, the conclusion is already known before combining the premises. Why? Because the premises are not connected, so you cannot infer a novel result. Real conditionals (luzūmiyya) have a necessary connection that generates new knowledge.
4
Contrast with luzūmiyya in Islamic sciences
In Islamic legal theory, for example, the conditional 'If wine is an intoxicant, it is forbidden' is luzūmiyya because the prohibition necessarily follows from the intoxicating property. That yields knowledge. A coincidental conditional like 'If wine is red, it is forbidden' has no logical force.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا الموضع على وجه تشعر بكيفياتها على سبيل التفصيل فنقول الحدود المذكورة في الاتفاقيات نحو الأصغر والأوسط والأكبر فإنه يمكن أن يقع كل واحد منها بالصدق، ويمكن أن لا يقع بل يقع كل واحد منها بالكذب أو البعض منها بالصدق والبعض بالكذب ؛ وحينئذ يلزم أن تكون المقدمتان قد تجتمعان بالصدق في كل حال كما يقال كلَّما كان الإنسان ناطقا كان الحمار ناهقا، وكلَّما كان الحمار ناهقًا كان الغراب ناعقا. وقد تجتمعان بالكذب كذلك كما يقال كلَّما كان الإنسان ناهقًا كان الحمار ناطقا، وكلَّما كان الحمار ناطقا كان الغراب ناطقا . وقد لا تجتمعان على كل واحد منهما بل عليهما كما يقال كلَّما كان الإنسان ناطقا كان الحمار ناهقا، وكلما كان الحمار ناهقا كان الأسد ناعقًا؛ وعلى هذا في السوالب. ثمَّ إنا إذا قلنا كلَّما كان الإنسان ناطقا كان الحمار ناهقا فهو صادق والصدق فيه ظاهر . فأما إذا قلنا كلما كان كل إنسان ناطقا كان كل حمار ناهقًا فليس كذلك؛ إذ لا يبعد أن يظن أنه كاذب وإن كان ذلك الظنّ باطلا ؛ فإن قولنا كل حمار ناهق لا يفتقر في الصدق على وجود الحمار حتى تكون الكثرة مانعة. ومنهم من زعم أنه يلزم من قولنا كلما كان كل إنسان ناهقًا أن يكون كل حمار ناطقاً، وبالعكس لكن لا يلزم البتة لا على سبيل اللزوم؛ وهذا ظاهر، ولا على سبيل الاتفاق حتى إذا فرض أن هذا صادقًا يمكن أن يوجد ذلك صادقا معه؛ وذلك من جملة ما لا يمكن صدقه أصلا. ولما عرفت هذا فلك أن تعرف بأن ما يكون معتبراً من التركيبات الشرطية فذلك ما يتركب من الشرطيات الحقيقية وهي اللزومية؛ فإن ما يتركب من الغير وهو الاتفاقية، فذلك من جملة ما يكون خاليا عن الإفادة. ولهذا لا يعتبر في المباحث العلمية بل لا يستعمل فيها لما أن العلم بالنتيجة حاصل قبل تركيب المقدمات وترتيبها من الاتفاقيات الصرفة.
This passage explains their modalities in detail. We say: the terms mentioned in coincidental conditionals, like the minor, middle, and major, can each be true, or can each be false, or some true and some false. Then the two premises may both be true in every case, as in: 'Whenever man is speaking, the donkey is braying; and whenever the donkey is braying, the crow is cawing.' And they may both be false similarly, as in: 'Whenever man is braying, the donkey is speaking; and whenever the donkey is speaking, the crow is speaking.' And they may not both be either but rather one true and one false, as in: 'Whenever man is speaking, the donkey is braying; and whenever the donkey is braying, the lion is roaring.' And similarly for negatives. Then if we say: 'Whenever man is speaking, the donkey is braying' it is true, and its truth is apparent. But if we say: 'Whenever every man is speaking, every donkey is braying' it is not so; for it is not unlikely that one would think it false, even if that notion is invalid; because our statement 'every donkey is braying' does not require for its truth the existence of the donkey such that multiplicity prevents it. And some claim that from our statement 'Whenever every man is braying' it follows that every donkey is speaking, and vice versa, but it does not follow at all, not by way of necessity—this is apparent—nor by way of coincidence such that if this is assumed true, that could be true with it; and that is among what cannot be true at all. And once you know this, you may know that what is considered valid among conditional compositions are those composed of real conditionals, which are necessitative; for what is composed of other, i.e., coincidental, is among what is devoid of benefit. Therefore it is not considered in scientific investigations, nor is it used there, because knowledge of the conclusion is obtained before combining and ordering the premises in pure coincidental conditionals.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage is a detailed classification of coincidental conditionals (ittifaqiyyat) and their truth conditions, followed by a decisive verdict on their scientific uselessness. The author first explains that in coincidental conditionals, the terms (like minor, middle, major) can be true or false in various combinations. He gives examples: both premises can be true together (e.g., whenever man is speaking, donkey brays; whenever donkey brays, crow caws), both false (man brays, donkey speaks; donkey speaks, crow speaks), or one true and one false (man speaks, donkey brays; donkey brays, lion roars). This applies equally to negative statements. Then the author addresses a subtle distinction: when the quantifier 'every' is added, as in 'Whenever every man is speaking, every donkey is braying,' the truth conditions change because the truth of 'every donkey is braying' depends on the existence of donkeys. He notes that some imagined a reciprocal entailment between such statements, but he refutes that: no entailment exists, either by necessity or even by coincidence. Finally, he concludes that only necessitative (luzumiyya) conditionals are valid in scientific discourse; coincidental conditionals are empty because their conclusions are already known before combining the premises. This is a key epistemological point: for a conditional syllogism to produce new knowledge, the connection must be real, not accidental.

Key Terms
اتفاقية
لزومية

Key Takeaway
Coincidental conditionals can be true, but they are epistemically barren—only necessitative conditionals drive the Islamic scientific enterprise.

Specific vs General Coincidental Conditionals
Author explains truth conditions, distinguishes specific/general, and critiques the distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between specific and general coincidental conditionals and explain why the distinction is unnecessary for logical inference.

Core Idea
The specific/general distinction in coincidentals is a logical refinement, but ultimately superfluous for valid inference.

In the last lesson, we encountered coincidental conditionals. Now we examine their truth conditions more closely and a subtle distinction some logicians draw. The author, building on Ibn Sina, argues this distinction is unnecessary and demonstrates why by analyzing the failure of conversion in universal negatives. This lesson sharpens your understanding of conditional logic and its limits.

Guided Path
1
1. Identify truth conditions for coincidental conditionals
For an affirmative coincidental conditional to be true, it suffices that the consequent is true regardless of the antecedent's truth. The antecedent may be true essentially or accidentally.
The truth of the consequent alone is sufficient.
2
2. Distinguish specific vs general
A 'specific coincidental' requires both antecedent and consequent to be true; a 'general coincidental' requires only the consequent. General is broader.
Some logicians name them as such. The author later critiques this distinction.
3
3. Analyze Ibn Sina's example on conversion
Ibn Sina's example: 'It is never the case that if man exists, void exists' is true, but its converse 'if void exists, man exists' is false. This shows universal negative coincidentals do not convert.
Conversion fails because man's existence is necessary, so void's existence does not imply man's non-existence.
4
4. Evaluate the critique of the distinction
The author argues that the majority of philosophers, including Ibn Sina, ignore the specific/general split. They treat all coincidentals uniformly because the distinction does not affect the validity of proofs.
Making the distinction is unnecessary effort (takalluf).

Source Text

And the coincidental, as mentioned in conditionals, suffices for the truth of the affirmative among them: the truth of the consequent, so that its truth is realized on the two suppositions of the truth of the antecedent in itself and its non-truth. The antecedent can be true in reality, as in our saying: 'Whenever man is speaking in his essence, the donkey is braying in its essence,' and it can be true accidentally, as in our saying: 'Whenever man is a horse, the lion is a visitor,' meaning no state is assumed for the antecedent except that the consequent is assumed in it. And it is hidden from no one that what requires the truth of the antecedent in itself, or rather the truth of both antecedent and consequent, is more specific relative to what requires only the truth of the consequent. And some named the more specific of them 'specific coincidental' and the more general 'general coincidental.' And for this reason, the Shaykh stated regarding the universal negative coincidental that it does not convert: for it is true to say 'It is never the case that if man exists, void exists'; but it is not at all true to say 'It is never the case that if void exists, man exists', because man is among things whose existence is necessary in reality; so the assumption of the existence of void in reality does not entail its non-existence. Then the majority of philosophers, and the Shaykh among them, did not pay attention to the distinction between the specific and the general among coincidentals; rather their attention was to the coincidental qua coincidental, whether specific or general. And their inferences by the proofs that indicate them, whether the premises used are general or specific, should not be thought to be inconsistent with their objectives. And it is known that the indicator of the general does not necessarily imply that it is an indicator of the specific; it can be with respect to the more general as it should be, even if not with respect to the more specific similarly. Then the distinction between the specific and the general made by others is unnecessary effort in coincidental compositions, because they...
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage delves deeper into the truth conditions of coincidental conditionals and introduces a distinction between 'specific' and 'general' coincidental conditionals. The author explains that for an affirmative coincidental conditional to be true, it is sufficient that the consequent is true when the antecedent is either true or false. The antecedent can be true in reality (e.g., 'man is speaking in his essence') or true accidentally (e.g., 'man is a horse,' which is impossible but serves as a hypothetical). The key point: if a conditional requires both the antecedent and consequent to be true, it is more specific than one requiring only the consequent. Some call this 'specific coincidental' versus 'general coincidental.' The author then provides an example from al-Shaykh (Ibn Sina) about the universal negative coincidental not converting: 'It is never the case that if man exists, void exists' is true because void does not exist in reality, but its converse 'if void exists, man exists' is false because man's existence is necessary, so the absence of man does not follow from the existence of void. This illustrates that conversion fails because the antecedent may be necessarily true. Then the author critiques the distinction: the majority of philosophers, including al-Shaykh, do not differentiate between specific and general coincidental; they treat all coincidentals the same and use them uniformly in demonstrations. The author argues that making this distinction is unnecessary effort (takalluf) because the indicator of the general can still indicate the specific appropriately. The passage ends abruptly, but the intended point is that the distinction is moot in practical scientific inference.

Agreement Conditionals: Truth Conditions and Types
Definition and classification of agreement conditionals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and classify agreement conditionals as general or specific, and evaluate the validity of their conversion.

Core Idea
An agreement conditional is true if the consequent is true, regardless of the antecedent.

Conditional propositions can be divided into those that imply a necessary connection (luzumiyya) and those that do not (ittifaqiyya). Understanding agreement conditionals is crucial for evaluating arguments where the connection is merely coincidental. This lesson unpacks the definition, subtypes, and a key conversion rule.

Guided Path
1
Identify the truth condition
The passage states that an affirmative agreement conditional is true if the consequent is true, regardless of whether the antecedent is true in itself or not. This is the central defining feature.
The truth of the antecedent is irrelevant for the truth of the conditional.
2
Distinguish essential vs. accidental antecedent truth
The antecedent may be essentially true (e.g., 'man is rational') or accidentally true (e.g., 'man is a horse'). In both cases, the conditional holds as long as the consequent is true.
Essential truth means the attribute is part of the essence; accidental truth is a false supposition used for illustration.
3
Distinguish general vs. specific agreement conditionals
A specific agreement conditional requires both the antecedent and consequent to be true in reality. A general agreement conditional requires only the consequent to be true.
The specific type is a subset of the general type, imposing an extra condition.
4
Apply the conversion rule for universal negatives
The passage gives the example: 'Absolutely not: if man exists, then void exists' is true, but its converse 'Absolutely not: if void exists, then man exists' is false. Therefore, universal negative agreement conditionals do not convert.
This is because the consequent (void existence) does not imply the antecedent (man existence) – man necessarily exists, so the converse would be false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والاتفاقية كما مرَّ ذكرها في الشرطيات تكفي في صدق الموجبة منها صدق التالي حتى يتحقق صدقها على تقديري صدق المقدم في نفسه وعدم الصدق. والمقدم قد يكون صادقا بالحقيقة كما في قولنا كلما كان الإنسان ناطقا في ماهيته كان الحمار ناهقًا في ماهيته، وقد يكون صادقا بالعرض كما في قولنا كلما كان الإنسان فرسا كان الأسد زائرا أي لا حال يفرض فيه المقدم إلا وأن يفرض فيه التالي. ولا خفاء في أن ما يفتقر إلى صدق المقدم في نفسه بل إلى صدق المقدم والتالي فهو أخص بالنسبة إلى ما لا يفتقر إلا إلى صدق التالي. ومنهم من سمى الأخص منهما اتفاقية خاصة، والأعم اتفاقية عامة. ولهذا المعنى صرَّح الشيخ في السالبة الكلية الاتفاقية إنها لا تنعكس فإنه يصدق أن يقال ليس البتة إذا كان الإنسان موجوداً كان الخلاء موجودا؛ ولا يصدق أصلا أن يقال ليس البتة إذا كان الخلاء موجودًا كان الإنسان موجودا؛ إذ الإنسان من جملة ما يجب وجوده في نفس الأمر؛ فلا يلزم من فرض وجود الخلاء في نفس الأمر عدم وجوده.
And the agreement conditional, as mentioned before in conditional propositions, suffices for the truth of its affirmative the truth of the consequent, so that its truth is realized upon the two suppositions of the antecedent being true in itself and not being true. The antecedent may be essentially true, as in our statement: 'Whenever man is rational in his essence, the donkey is braying in its essence,' and it may be accidentally true, as in our statement: 'Whenever man is a horse, the lion is visiting,' i.e., there is no state in which the antecedent is supposed except that the consequent is supposed. And it is not hidden that what depends on the truth of the antecedent in itself, rather on the truth of the antecedent and the consequent, is more specific relative to what depends only on the truth of the consequent. And among them are those who call the more specific of the two a specific agreement conditional, and the more general a general agreement conditional. And for this meaning, the Shaykh explicitly stated concerning the universal negative agreement conditional that it does not convert; for it is true to say: 'Absolutely not: if man exists, then void exists,' but it is not at all true to say: 'Absolutely not: if void exists, then man exists,' because man is among those things whose existence is necessary in reality; so the supposition of the existence of void in reality does not necessitate his non-existence.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies agreement conditionals (ittifaqiyya). The author begins by restating that an affirmative agreement conditional is true if the consequent is true, regardless of the antecedent's truth in itself. Two examples illustrate: an essential truth (man rational, donkey braying) and an accidental truth (man as a horse, lion visiting). The key distinction emerges: if a conditional requires both antecedent and consequent to be true, it is 'specific' (khassa); if it requires only the consequent true, it is 'general' ('amma). The Shaykh (Ibn Sina) then provides a conversion example: 'Whenever man exists, void exists' is false as a universal negative; but 'Whenever void exists, man does not exist' is false because man necessarily exists. This shows that universal negative agreement conditionals do not convert simply. The passage thus provides both definition and a rule about conversion.

Definitions of General and Specific in Conditional Entailment
Definitions of 'amm and khass and basic entailment rules

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which term is general and which is specific in a conditional proposition based on the direction of entailment.

Core Idea
The specific entails the general; the general is entailed by the specific.

In Islamic logic, the terms 'amm and khass describe a relation of one-way entailment between two propositions. Understanding this distinction is crucial for analyzing conditional arguments in fiqh and kalam. The passage defines these terms and establishes the basic rules of their interaction.

Guided Path
1
Identify antecedent and consequent
Locate the mقدم and تالي in the conditional statement. For example, in 'If A then B', A is antecedent, B is consequent.
2
Determine entailment direction
Check which term, if true, necessarily makes the other true. The term that entails the other is the specific (khass); the term that is entailed is the general ('amm).
3
Label the terms
The specific entails the general without converse. The general is entailed by the specific without converse.
4
Verify the rules
The existence of the general does not imply the existence of the specific, and the non-existence of the specific does not imply the non-existence of the general.
5
Apply to Islamic examples
For instance, in the conditional 'If it is wine (khamr), then it is intoxicating', 'wine' is specific and 'intoxicating' is general, because wine entails intoxication but intoxication does not entail wine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فكذلك ولا نقيضه أيضًا لاحتمال أن يكون التالي أعم من المقدم. ولا يلزم من وجود العام وجود الخاص وإلا لكان قادحًا في كونه أعم، ولا من عدم الخاص عدم العام كذلك وإلا لكان قادحًا في كونه أخص. وقد مرّ من قبل أن الخاص عبارة عما يكون مستلزما للغير من غير عكس، والعام عبارة عما يكون لازما للغير من غير عكس فمنهم من قال: «إن الملازمة إذا كانت تامة كما إذا كان اللازم مساويًا للملزوم كاستثناء نقيض المقدم يوجب نقيض التالي، واستثناء عين التالي يوجب عين المقدم كذلك»، غير أنه خطأ؛ فإن اللزوم في كل الصورة من الجانبين. ولو كان كذلك لكان استثناء كل واحد منهما إما استثناء عين المقدم أو استثناء نقيض التالي لما أن الشرطية كما تكون متحققة من هذا الجانب من ذلك الجانب. ولا يظن أن الملازمة من الجانبين شرطية واحدة، والشرطية كما مرَّ هي الحكم بلزوم قضية أو لا لزومها لقضية أخرى. وهذا الحكم كما يكون من هذا الجانب فكذلك من ذلك. ثمَّ استثناء عين المقدم لعين التالي بين بذاته في جميع الصور بخلاف استثناء نقيض التالي لنقيض المقدم ؛ إذ هو بواسطة ذلك لما أن الشيء لا يكون ممتنعا بدون الغير إلا وأن يكون عدم الغير ممتنعا بدون عدمه. فاستلزام وجود هذا لوجود ذلك مستلزم لاستلزام عدم ذلك لعدمه ومستلزم لاستلزام وجود أحدهما لوجود ذلك؛ ولاستلزام عدم أحدهما لعدم هذا كذلك. ولا مجال للملازمة بين الشيئين إلا وأن يتحقق أحد هذين الأمرين وهو وجود اللازم أو عدم الملزوم لما أن الملزوم لا يخلو من أن يكون أو لا يكون؛ وكذلك اللازم. ثم المتّصلة التي حكمنا عليها بالأحكام المذكورة يجب أن تكون موجبة فإنها إذا كانت سالبة كان سلب الاتصال بين الأمرين مع العلم بوجود أحدهما لا يوجب العلم بوجود الآخر ، ولا بعدم أحدهما لا يوجب العلم بعدم الآخر كذلك.
And likewise, nor its opposite, because it is possible that the consequent is more general than the antecedent. The existence of the general does not necessitate the existence of the specific, otherwise it would be detrimental to its being general, nor does the non-existence of the specific necessitate the non-existence of the general, otherwise it would be detrimental to its being more specific. It has previously passed that the specific is that which entails another without the converse, and the general is that which is entailed by another without the converse. Some have said: 'If the entailment is complete, as when the necessary is equal to the necessitated, like the exclusion of the opposite of the antecedent necessitates the opposite of the consequent, and the exclusion of the very consequent necessitates the very antecedent likewise,' but this is a mistake; for the entailment in every form is from both sides. If it were so, then excluding each one of them would either be excluding the very antecedent or the opposite of the consequent, because the conditional, as it is actualized from this side, is from that side. And one should not think that the entailment from both sides is one conditional; the conditional, as has passed, is the judgment of the necessity of a proposition or its non-necessity to another proposition. And this judgment, as it is from this side, is likewise from that. Then, the exclusion of the very antecedent for the very consequent is self-evident in all forms, unlike the exclusion of the opposite of the consequent for the opposite of the antecedent; for it is by virtue of that, because a thing is not impossible without the other unless the non-existence of the other is impossible without its non-existence. So the necessitation of the existence of this for the existence of that necessitates the necessitation of the non-existence of that for its non-existence, and necessitates the necessitation of the existence of one of them for the existence of the other; and the necessitation of the non-existence of one of them for the non-existence of the other likewise. And there is no room for entailment between two things unless one of these two matters is realized, which is the existence of the necessary or the non-existence of the necessitating, because the necessitating cannot but be either existent or non-existent; and likewise the necessary. Then, the conditional which we have judged with the mentioned rulings must be affirmative, for if it is negative, the negation of connection between the two matters, with knowledge of the existence of one of them, does not necessitate knowledge of the existence of the other, nor does the non-existence of one of them necessitate knowledge of the non-existence of the other likewise.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the rules of entailment (luzum) in conditional propositions, specifically addressing the relationship between general (عام) and specific (خاص) terms. The author begins by clarifying a common point: if the consequent (تالي) is more general than the antecedent (مقدم), then the absence of the antecedent does not entail the absence of the consequent, and the existence of the antecedent does not entail the existence of the consequent in all cases. This is because the general is wider and can exist without the specific. The author then defines the specific as that which entails another thing without the converse (i.e., it is more restrictive), and the general as that which is entailed by another without the converse (i.e., it is broader). Some scholars mistakenly claimed that if entailment is complete (ملازمة تامة), such as when the necessary (لازم) equals the necessitated (ملزوم) in scope, then the rule of contraposition (exclusion of the opposite of the antecedent gives the opposite of the consequent, and exclusion of the very consequent gives the very antecedent) holds. The author refutes this, stating that entailment is always from both sides; the conditional judgment applies to both directions. He explains that the exclusion of the very antecedent for the very consequent is straightforward, but the exclusion of the opposite of the consequent for the opposite of the antecedent requires justification: the impossibility of a thing without another implies the impossibility of the other's absence without its own absence. This is the basis of contraposition. The author then asserts that there can be no entailment between two things unless either the necessary exists or the necessitating does not exist. Finally, he states that for a conditional to be used in syllogisms, it must be affirmative, because a negative conditional (one that denies connection) does not allow inference of the existence or non-existence of one part from the other. For example, in usul al-fiqh, if we say 'If the water is pure, then the prayer is valid', this is an affirmative conditional; but if we say 'It is not the case that if the water is pure then the prayer is valid,' then knowing the water is pure gives no knowledge about the prayer's validity.

الخاص
الملازمة

D) They necessitate each other.

Key Takeaway
The specific entails the general; the general does not entail the specific.

The Mistake of Complete Entailment and the Correct Rule of Contraposition
Refutation of a mistaken view and justification of contraposition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to refute the claim that complete entailment allows symmetrical inference and correctly apply contraposition in conditional syllogisms.

Core Idea
Entailment is always from both sides; the mistake is thinking that complete entailment gives symmetrical inference.

Some logicians erroneously believed that when the necessary and necessitated are equal in scope, both modus ponens and modus tollens are valid in both directions. Our author corrects this, clarifying that while entailment is from both sides, the conditional judgment is a single judgment of necessity, and contraposition requires a specific justification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mistaken claim
Some have said: 'If the entailment is complete, as when the necessary is equal to the necessitated, then excluding the opposite of the antecedent necessitates the opposite of the consequent, and excluding the very consequent necessitates the very antecedent.'
This appears in the passage as 'فمنهم من قال: إن الملازمة إذا كانت تامة...'
2
Understand the author's refutation
The author says this is a mistake because entailment is from both sides; the conditional is a single judgment of necessity. The claimed symmetry would mean that every exclusion is either of the very antecedent or the opposite of the consequent, which conflates the direction of entailment with the direction of inference.
3
Analyze the correct argument for contraposition
Contraposition is valid because the impossibility of a thing without another implies the impossibility of the other's absence without its own absence. This is the basis for inferring 'if not B then not A' from 'if A then B'.
4
Apply the rule
Given a conditional 'If A then B', the contrapositive is 'If not B then not A'. This is valid only if the original conditional is affirmative and the entailment is understood correctly.

Classification of Premises by Modality and Reputation
Author classifies premises by modality and reputation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between real and reputational premises and identify the modality types mentioned in the passage.

Core Idea
Premises vary by modality and epistemic status; 'generally accepted' may not be true in reality.

In constructing a syllogism, not all premises are equal. Some are necessary, others possible; some are true in reality, others only by reputation. The author guides us to recognise these distinctions and notes that each science has its own appropriate premises.

Guided Path
1
Identify the modality types
List the types mentioned: necessary (wājib), possible (mumkin), absolute (muṭlaq), perpetual (dā'im), and their negations. Note that the author says no need to investigate them in detail here.
These modal categories affect the strength of the syllogism.
2
Distinguish real vs. reputational
Affirmation/negation and necessity/non-necessity can be either real (bi'l-ḥaqīqa) or by reputation (bi'l-shuhra). Understand that 'by reputation' means generally accepted, not necessarily true in reality.
This distinction is crucial in dialectic and demonstration.
3
Differentiate two kinds of mashhur
The generally accepted (mashhūr) has two types: genuinely universally accepted (mashhūr bi'l-ḥaqīqa) and accepted at first sight (mashhūr fī bādī al-ra'y). The latter may be false upon reflection.
Only the first type is reliable for demonstration.
4
Recognize the connection to sciences
Each science (ṣinā'a) has its own specific premises. Acquiring them requires reflection on that science and experience. The absolute syllogism demands distinguishing all these types.
Further details belong to the art of dialectic (jadal).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم من المقدمات ما يكون واجبا، ومنها ما يكون ممكنا، ومنها ما يكون مطلقا، ومنها ما لا يكون، ومنها ما يكون دائما، ومنها ما لا يكون: وهذه كلها من جملة ما لا حاجة إلى البحث عنه في هذا الموضع. وأما السلب والحمل فكما أن كل واحد منهما قد يكون بالحقيقة، وقد يكون بالشهرة فكذلك الضرورة واللا ضرورة. والمشهور أيضًا منه ما هو مشهور بالحقيقة، ومنه ما هو مشهور في بادي الرأي؛ فأنتَ من حيث تكتسب القياس المطلق يجب أن تميز جميع ذلك، ويجب أن تعلم أن لكل صناعة مقدمات خاصة، فتكون اللواحق والملحوقات. وما لا يلحق إنما يطلب بحسب تأمل تلك الصناعة فإن إدراكها كما ينبغي لا يحصل إلا بالتفطن فيما يتعلق بها؛ ثم بالتجربة فهذه إشارة إلى اكتساب القياس ومقدماته، وأما تفصيلها فذلك يعرف في صناعة الجدل إن شاء الله تعالى.
Then among premises are what is necessary, and among them what is possible, and among them what is absolute, and among them what is not, and among them what is perpetual, and among them what is not: and all these are among what there is no need to investigate in this place. As for negation and affirmation, just as each of them may be in reality or by reputation, similarly necessity and non-necessity. Also the generally accepted among them is what is generally accepted in reality, and among them what is generally accepted at first sight. So you, from the perspective of acquiring the absolute syllogism, must distinguish all that, and you must know that every science has specific premises, which are the properties and the things that are properties of. And what is not a property is sought only by reflection on that science; for their correct understanding is not obtained except by attention to what pertains to them, then by experience. So this is an indication of the acquisition of syllogism and its premises. As for their detail, that is known in the art of dialectic, God willing.
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage is a brief classification of premises according to modality and epistemic status. The author lists several modality types: necessary (wājib), possible (mumkin), absolute (muṭlaq), perpetual (dā'im), and their negations. However, he states that these are not needed in detail here. He then notes that affirmation and negation (haml and salb) can be either real (bi'l-ḥaqīqa) or based on reputation (bi'l-shuhra). Similarly, necessity and non-necessity can be real or reputational. Moreover, 'generally accepted' (mashhūr) can be genuinely universally accepted or only accepted at first sight (fī bādī al-ra'y). The author stresses that when constructing an absolute syllogism, one must distinguish these, and that each science (ṣinā'a) has its own special premises. The acquisition of these premises requires reflection on the science and experience. He concludes that the details belong to the art of dialectic (jadal).

Premises: Modality and Discipline-Specificity
Author classifies premises by modality and discipline.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify premises by modality (necessary, possible, absolute, perpetual) and explain why each Islamic discipline requires its own set of premises for syllogistic reasoning.

Core Idea
Premises vary in modality and source; each discipline has its own premises.

The author of this passage stresses that premises are not all the same. They differ in modality—some are necessary, some possible, some absolute, some perpetual—and also in source: some are true in reality, others only by reputation. Moreover, each Islamic science (fiqh, kalam, etc.) has its own foundational premises. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for constructing valid syllogisms and for assessing the strength of conclusions. This lesson will guide you through the classification of premises and its practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify premise types
List the modality terms mentioned in the passage: wājib (necessary), mumkin (possible), mutlaq (absolute), dā'im (perpetual), and their negations (e.g., 'what is not perpetual'). Also note the categories of negation and affirmation (salb wa ḥaml).
These terms are standard in Aristotelian logic but here applied to propositions.
2
Understand the meaning of each type
Define each: Necessary (must be true), Possible (could be true), Absolute (no modality restriction), Perpetual (holds at all times). Recognize that the author says there is no need to investigate them here—they are covered elsewhere (e.g., in discussions of modality).
This classification affects the certainty of the conclusion.
3
Distinguish real vs. reputed premises
The passage contrasts premises that are 'true in reality' (bi-l-ḥaqīqa) with those that are 'by reputation' (bi-l-shuhra). The well-known (mashhur) can be genuinely well-known or only seemingly so at first glance (bādī al-ra’y).
A premise accepted by reputation may not be certain; this is crucial in dialectical arguments.
4
Recognize discipline-specific premises
The passage states: 'each discipline (ṣinā‘a) has its own premises.' For example, the premises of fiqh differ from those of kalām. A syllogism in fiqh must use premises drawn from the sources of fiqh (Qur’an, Sunnah, consensus, analogy).
Transplanting premises across disciplines can invalidate the argument.
5
Apply to the acquisition of syllogism
The author concludes that one must distinguish all these types when acquiring the absolute syllogism. Proper perception requires attentiveness and experience within the discipline. This is just an indication; detailed treatment is in the art of dialectic (jadal).
The classification is not merely theoretical; it has practical consequences for reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أيضًا قد يكتسب بهذا النحو وذلك يتبع لواحق حدود النقيض وملحوقاتها، ويمكن أن يكتسب أيضًا من هذا المأخذ ما يفتقر إليه في الاستثناء، ويمكن أن يكتسب الاستقراء أيضًا بل يمكن أن يكتسب قياسًا كليا يكتسب الجزئي بقوة الانعكاس.
ثم من المقدمات ما يكون واجبا، ومنها ما يكون ممكنا، ومنها ما يكون مطلقا، ومنها ما لا يكون، ومنها ما يكون دائما، ومنها ما لا يكون: وهذه كلها من جملة ما لا حاجة إلى البحث عنه في هذا الموضع. وأما السلب والحمل فكما أن كل واحد منهما قد يكون بالحقيقة، وقد يكون بالشهرة فكذلك الضرورة واللا ضرورة. والمشهور أيضًا منه ما هو مشهور بالحقيقة، ومنه ما هو مشهور في بادي الرأي؛ فأنتَ من حيث تكتسب القياس المطلق يجب أن تميز جميع ذلك، ويجب أن تعلم أن لكل صناعة مقدمات خاصة، فتكون اللواحق والملحوقات. وما لا يلحق إنما يطلب بحسب تأمل تلك الصناعة فإن إدراكها كما ينبغي لا يحصل إلا بالتفطن فيما يتعلق بها؛ ثم بالتجربة فهذه إشارة إلى اكتساب القياس ومقدماته، وأما تفصيلها فذلك يعرف في صناعة الجدل إن شاء الله تعالى.
Also, it may be acquired in this manner, and that follows the concomitants of the definitions of the opposite and their attachments. It can also be acquired from this source what is needed in the exception, and induction can also be acquired, rather a universal syllogism can be acquired by which the particular is acquired through the power of conversion.
Then some premises are necessary, some are possible, some are absolute, some are not, some are perpetual, some are not: and all of these are among those that there is no need to investigate in this place. As for negation and affirmation, just as each of them may be in reality and may be by reputation, similarly necessity and non-necessity. The well-known also includes what is well-known in reality and what is well-known at first glance. So you, in acquiring the absolute syllogism, must distinguish all of that, and you must know that each discipline has its own premises, so the concomitants and the attached. What is not attached is sought only by contemplation of that discipline, for its proper perception is only achieved by being attentive to what pertains to it, then by experience. So this is an indication of the acquisition of the syllogism and its premises; as for their detail, that is known in the art of dialectic, God willing.
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage is a methodological discussion on acquiring syllogisms and their premises. The author begins by noting that premises can be acquired in various ways, including through the concomitants of opposites and through conversion. He then enumerates types of premises based on modality: necessary, possible, absolute, perpetual, etc., but states that these are not the focus here. He distinguishes between premises that are true in reality and those that are true by reputation (mashhur). The key point is that one must distinguish these types when acquiring an absolute syllogism. Moreover, each discipline (such as fiqh or kalam) has its own specific premises and their concomitants. Proper understanding of these comes through attentiveness and experience. The passage concludes by saying this is just an indication; detailed treatment is in the art of dialectic (jadal).

Three Meanings of the Thing in the Mind
Author distinguishes three interpretations of a proposition about mental existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three interpretations of a statement about mental existence and identify which leads to falsehood and which to a deficiency in expression.

Core Idea
Ambiguity in mental existence claims yields false propositions or deficient expression.

When a logician says 'the thing whose image exists in the mind is eternal,' the meaning is not obvious. The author of our passage dissects this statement into three possible interpretations and evaluates each. This lesson will train you to spot such ambiguity in propositions about mental existence, a common source of error in theological and philosophical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ambiguous proposition
Find the phrase 'the thing whose image is in the mind exists in the external world always.' Recognize that this can be interpreted in multiple ways depending on what 'exists' refers to.
2
List the three meanings
The author proposes three meanings: (1) the external thing itself always exists, (2) the mental image always remains, (3) one makes a judgment in the mind that the external thing is eternal. Write them down.
The first two focus on actuality, the third on a mental judgment.
3
Evaluate truth of each meaning
According to the passage, the first and second meanings yield falsehood because external things are temporary and mental images may not persist. The third meaning does not make the proposition false, but it reveals a deficiency in expression.
Distinguish between falsehood and expressive deficiency.
4
Examine the consequence of the third meaning
The author adds that if one interprets 'the imagined Zayd is eternal in the judgment of the mind,' then the individual Zayd must also be eternal in that result, which leads to a problematic conclusion. This shows how ambiguous expression can lead to logical pitfalls.
This step anticipates the ripple effect of imprecise language.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الألفاظ فذلك أن الشيء الحاصل صورته في الوهم موجود في الأعيان دائما. والذي يطابق المعنى الذي هو أن الشيء الحاصل في الوهم صورته تبقى صورته في الوهم دائما بقي هو أو لم يبق . والذي يطابق المعنى الثالث هو أن الشيء الفلاني قد أحضر في الوهم صورته وحكم على أنها دائمة الوجود في الأعيان حكما في الوهم فإن عني به المعنى الأول أو الثاني فالقضية كاذبة، وإن عني به الثالث فهناك تقصير في العبارة؛ إذ معنى القول إن المتوهم زيد أزلي في حكم الوهم فيجب أن يوجد الأدنى كذلك في النتيجة فتكون النتيجة صادقة.
The expressions: that the thing whose image is in the mind exists in the external world always. That which corresponds to the meaning that the thing whose image is in the mind, its image remains in the mind always, whether it remains or not. And that which corresponds to the third meaning is that such-and-such thing has had its image brought to the mind and it has been judged that it is perpetually existent in the external world, a judgment in the mind. If the first or second meaning is intended, the proposition is false; and if the third is intended, then there is a deficiency in the expression; for the meaning of the statement 'the imagined Zayd is eternal in the judgment of the mind' is that the lower must also exist in the result, so the result is true.
Source pages p. 302

Commentary
This passage critiques a claim about mental existence. The author identifies three possible meanings of the statement 'the thing whose image is in the mind exists in the external world always.' The first meaning is that the external thing exists permanently; the second is that its mental image persists whether or not the external thing remains; the third is that one judges in the mind that the external thing is eternal. If the first or second is intended, the proposition is false because external things are temporary. If the third is intended, the expression is deficient because it conflates a mental judgment with actual existence. The author then points out a consequent issue: if one says 'the imagined Zayd is eternal in the judgment of the mind,' then a lower term (like a specific Zayd) would also have to be eternal in the result, making the conclusion true—but this is problematic. The passage thus guards against ambiguity in logical analysis.

تقصير في العبارة

C) The first or second meaning (the external thing is always existent, or its image always remains).
D) All meanings make it false.

Key Takeaway
A proposition about mental existence can have multiple meanings; only by distinguishing them can we assess truth or identify expressive deficiency.

Identifying Prepositions as Part of the Predicate
Author teaches how to detect prepositions that are part of the predicate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a preposition in a proposition is part of the predicate or a separate indicator, using the author's test of rephrasing.

Core Idea
Prepositions can be part of the predicate or not; rephrasing reveals the difference.

When you encounter a proposition with a preposition, the logical status of that preposition determines how you handle it in inference. The author provides a practical test: if you can rephrase the proposition so the preposition disappears and the meaning stays intact, it is not part of the predicate. This lesson will walk you through that test with examples from the passage.

Guided Path
1
Locate the preposition in the proposition
Identify the preposition (e.g., 'li' (لام), 'fi' (في)) in the proposition. In 'al-khayr mawjud lil-hikma' (good exists for wisdom), the preposition is 'li' in 'lil-hikma'.
2
Try to rephrase without the preposition
Rephrase the proposition to remove the preposition. For 'good exists for wisdom', you can say 'wisdom has good' (al-hikma laha khayr). If the meaning is preserved, the preposition is not part of the predicate.
The author shows that 'lam' in 'lil-hikma' is not part of the predicate, because you can dispense with it.
3
Check if a direct rephrasing changes the meaning
If removing the preposition changes the logical relation (e.g., from 'existence in' to 'ownership'), the preposition might be essential. For 'good exists for wisdom', rephrasing to 'wisdom has good' is acceptable; no change in truth condition.
If the preposition is part of the predicate, it cannot be removed without altering the sense.
4
Apply the test to a new example from the passage
Consider 'ilm wahid fi al-addad' (one knowledge in opposites). Can you say 'al-addad laha ilm wahid' (opposites have one knowledge)? The author says you need 'fi' or its equivalent, indicating that 'fi' here is part of the predicate.
If the preposition is essential, it must remain as part of the term.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإذا وجدتَ شيئًا من هذا الجنس فانظر هل هو جزء؛ فاحتفظه جزءا، وأضفه إلى ما هو الأقرب واجعل منهما حدا واحدا، واطرح الآخر، واقلب القضية على عبارة لا يحوِّجك إلى استعمال ذلك. فإنك إذا قلت الخير موجود للحكمة احتجت إلى هذا الحرف وهو اللام، وإذا قلت للحكمة خير استغنيت عنه؛ تعلم أن حرف اللام ليس جزءًا من المحمول أصلا. فأما إذا قلتَ علم واحد في الأضداد ثمَّ قلت الأضداد فيها علم واحد فلك حاجة إلى لفظة «في» أو «ما» يقوم معا فيها. وقد سبق أن يكون مثل هذه اللفظة في الصغرى والكبرى جميعًا دالة على الجزء كما في قولك كل كيفية فيها تصديق، وكل ما فيه تصديق فله موضوع؛ فكل كيفية له موضوع وربَّما اختلف الأمر في المقدمتين فكان الدال على الجزء في الكبرى، والآخر في الصغرى كقولك العلم موجود في كل كيفية، والكيفية موجودة في كل بياض. وربَّما كان بالعكس كقولك الخير موجود في العلم والعلم موجود في كل كيفية؛ وليس هذا في الإيجاب حده بل في السلب أيضًا كقولك ليس في الكون كون، وليس للحركة حركة. فإن كل واحد من الحرفين إذا كان دالا على الحمل كانت كل واحدة من القضيتين كاذبة، وإن كان كل واحد منهما جزءا من المحمول كانت صادقة؛ فإنك إذا قلت لا شيء من الكون موجودًا للكون، والكون موجود في كل لذة فقد أخذتَ اللام في الكبرى جزءًا ينتج أن اللذة ليس لها كون وهو حق، وكل لذة كون، والكون ليس كون. وإذا أخذت دالا على الحمل لم يصح قولك أن الكون ليس موجودا للغير، وليس محمولا عليها. وكذلك العلامة لیست موجودة للعلامة موجودة للصحة وكذلك الوقت موجود الله سبحانه وتعالى؛ وهذه الاشياء تُخلصك قول المقدّمات على الترتيب المستعمل. فإن وضع الحدود وإن كان يحوِّجك أحيانًا إلى أن تقول إن الحيوان على الإنسان، والإنسان على الكاتب؛ فاستعمال المقدمات لا يحوِّجك إليه فتقول كل كاتب إنسان، وكل إنسان كاتب. واعلم أنه ربما صدق القول مرسلًا فإذا زِيدَ شرط كذب فإنه صادق إن الإنسان حساس، وليس بصادق إنه حسّاس النفس. وربَّما كذب مرسلًا كقولك إن الإنسان معدوم؛ فإن قيل معدوم النظير صدق. وأما ما لا يكون كذلك فإنه يصدق مرسلًا وغير مرسل كما أن الشيء إذا كان مملوكا لزيد فهو مملوك لا محالة. وأما صدق الشيء بسيطا دون المركب ومركب دون البسيط فقد مرَّ ذكره من قبل.
When you find something of this kind, look whether it is a part; retain it as a part, add it to what is nearest, make them one term, and discard the other, and convert the proposition to a phrase that does not require you to use that. For if you say 'good exists for wisdom' you need this letter, which is the lam, but if you say 'wisdom has good' you dispense with it; thus you know that the letter lam is not part of the predicate at all. As for when you say 'one knowledge exists in opposites' then say 'opposites have one knowledge', you need the word 'in' or something parallel to it. And it has already been mentioned that such a word appears in both the minor and major premises as indicating the part, as in your saying 'every quality has belief', and 'everything that has belief has a subject'; so every quality has a subject. Sometimes the matter differs in the two premises, so the part-indicator is in the major, and the other in the minor, as in your saying 'knowledge exists in every quality', and 'quality exists in every whiteness'. Sometimes it is the reverse, as in your saying 'good exists in knowledge' and 'knowledge exists in every quality'. And this is not only in affirmative but also in negative, as in your saying 'not in the universe is universe', and 'not to motion is motion'. For if each of the two letters indicates predication, each of the two propositions is false; but if each of them is part of the predicate, they are true. For if you say 'nothing of the universe exists for the universe', and 'the universe exists in every pleasure', you have taken the lam in the major as a part, resulting in that pleasure has no universe, and that is true; and every pleasure is a universe, and the universe is not universe. And if you take it as indicating predication, your statement that the universe does not exist for another is not valid, and it is not predicated on it. Similarly, the sign is not existent for the sign existent for health, and likewise time is existent for Allah, glory be to Him. And these things deliver you from stating the premises in the customary order. For although placing the definitions sometimes requires you to say 'animal is on human' and 'human is on writer', using the premises does not require you to do that; rather you say 'every writer is human, every human is animal'. And know that sometimes a statement is true absolutely, but when a condition is added it becomes false. For it is true that man is sentient, but it is not true that he is sentient-soul. And sometimes it is false absolutely, as in your saying 'man is non-existent'; if it is said 'non-existent in counterpart', it is true. As for what is not like that, it is true absolutely and conditionally, just as when something is owned by Zayd, it is owned without doubt. As for the truth of a thing simply without composition, or composite without simple, its mention has preceded.
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on prepositions in propositions. The author advises: when you find a preposition, see if it is part of the predicate; if so, retain it as part, add it to the nearest, and make them one term, dropping the other, and rephrase the proposition to avoid the preposition if possible. He gives an example: 'good exists for wisdom'—you need the 'lam' if you keep the phrase, but if you rephrase to 'wisdom has good', you avoid it, showing the lam is not part of the predicate. Then he discusses cases where the preposition is part of the predicate in both premises, as in 'every quality has belief' and 'everything that has belief has a subject'—here 'fi' is part of the predicate. Sometimes the preposition differs between premises, e.g. 'knowledge exists in every quality' and 'quality exists in every whiteness'—the part-indicator is in the major, and the other in the minor. He also covers negatives: 'not in the universe is universe' and 'not to motion is motion'. Next, he examines truth conditions: if the preposition indicates predication (haml), the proposition is false; if it is part of the predicate, it is true. For example, 'nothing of the universe exists for the universe'—if 'lam' is taken as part of the predicate, the conclusion 'pleasure has no universe' is true; if taken as predication, it fails. The author also discusses ordering premises: instead of 'animal is on human' and 'human is on writer', use 'every writer is human, every human is animal' by conversion. Finally, he discusses absolute vs. conditional statements: sometimes a statement is true absolutely but false when conditioned, e.g. 'man is sentient' true, but 'man is sentient soul' false; and vice versa: 'man is non-existent' false absolutely, but 'man is non-existent in counterpart' true; some statements are true both simply and conditionally, like 'something owned by Zayd is owned'.

Key Terms
جزء المحمول (juz' al-mahmul): Part of the predicate; a preposition that is essential to the meaning of the predicate term.
دال على الحمل (daall 'ala al-haml): Indicating predication; a preposition that serves only to connect the subject and predicate without being part of either.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) العلم موجود في كل كيفية

Key Takeaway
A preposition in a proposition may be part of the predicate or a pure connector; test by rephrasing.

Converting Propositions with Prepositions to Standard Form
Techniques to convert propositions avoiding prepositions and reorder premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to convert a proposition containing a non-predicate preposition into a form that eliminates the preposition, and reorder premises in a syllogism to standard categorical form.

Core Idea
Rephrasing and reordering premises frees reasoning from unnecessary prepositions.

After determining that a preposition is not part of the predicate, you can often convert the proposition to a simpler form that avoids the preposition entirely. The author also shows how to restructure syllogisms, such as changing 'animal is on human' to 'every human is animal', to achieve the standard subject-predicate order. This lesson will guide you through these conversions.

Guided Path
1
Identify a proposition with a non-predicate preposition
Select a proposition where the preposition is not part of the predicate, e.g., 'good exists for wisdom' (al-khayr mawjud lil-hikma). The 'lam' is not part of the predicate.
2
Convert to a form without the preposition
Rephrase the proposition so that the preposition disappears. For example, change 'good exists for wisdom' to 'wisdom has good' (al-hikma laha khayr). The new form uses a possessive construction.
The new proposition must be logically equivalent to the original.
3
Apply to negative propositions
The conversion also works for negatives. For 'not to motion is motion' (laysa lil-haraka haraka), you can say 'motion is not owned by motion' or simply 'motion is not for motion' but often better to rephrase to 'no motion exists for motion'.
The goal is to remove the preposition from the logical structure.
4
Reorder premises in a syllogism
Sometimes the customary order of premises may use prepositions. The author mentions that instead of 'animal is on human' and 'human is on writer', you can convert each to the standard 'every human is animal' and 'every writer is human' by conversion.
This reordering simplifies the syllogism and avoids non-standard expressions.
