فإذا وجدتَ شيئًا من هذا الجنس فانظر هل هو جزء؛ فاحتفظه جزءا، وأضفه إلى ما هو الأقرب واجعل منهما حدا واحدا، واطرح الآخر، واقلب القضية على عبارة لا يحوِّجك إلى استعمال ذلك. فإنك إذا قلت الخير موجود للحكمة احتجت إلى هذا الحرف وهو اللام، وإذا قلت للحكمة خير استغنيت عنه؛ تعلم أن حرف اللام ليس جزءًا من المحمول أصلا. فأما إذا قلتَ علم واحد في الأضداد ثمَّ قلت الأضداد فيها علم واحد فلك حاجة إلى لفظة «في» أو «ما» يقوم معا فيها. وقد سبق أن يكون مثل هذه اللفظة في الصغرى والكبرى جميعًا دالة على الجزء كما في قولك كل كيفية فيها تصديق، وكل ما فيه تصديق فله موضوع؛ فكل كيفية له موضوع وربَّما اختلف الأمر في المقدمتين فكان الدال على الجزء في الكبرى، والآخر في الصغرى كقولك العلم موجود في كل كيفية، والكيفية موجودة في كل بياض. وربَّما كان بالعكس كقولك الخير موجود في العلم والعلم موجود في كل كيفية؛ وليس هذا في الإيجاب حده بل في السلب أيضًا كقولك ليس في الكون كون، وليس للحركة حركة. فإن كل واحد من الحرفين إذا كان دالا على الحمل كانت كل واحدة من القضيتين كاذبة، وإن كان كل واحد منهما جزءا من المحمول كانت صادقة؛ فإنك إذا قلت لا شيء من الكون موجودًا للكون، والكون موجود في كل لذة فقد أخذتَ اللام في الكبرى جزءًا ينتج أن اللذة ليس لها كون وهو حق، وكل لذة كون، والكون ليس كون. وإذا أخذت دالا على الحمل لم يصح قولك أن الكون ليس موجودا للغير، وليس محمولا عليها. وكذلك العلامة لیست موجودة للعلامة موجودة للصحة وكذلك الوقت موجود الله سبحانه وتعالى؛ وهذه الاشياء تُخلصك قول المقدّمات على الترتيب المستعمل. فإن وضع الحدود وإن كان يحوِّجك أحيانًا إلى أن تقول إن الحيوان على الإنسان، والإنسان على الكاتب؛ فاستعمال المقدمات لا يحوِّجك إليه فتقول كل كاتب إنسان، وكل إنسان كاتب. واعلم أنه ربما صدق القول مرسلًا فإذا زِيدَ شرط كذب فإنه صادق إن الإنسان حساس، وليس بصادق إنه حسّاس النفس. وربَّما كذب مرسلًا كقولك إن الإنسان معدوم؛ فإن قيل معدوم النظير صدق. وأما ما لا يكون كذلك فإنه يصدق مرسلًا وغير مرسل كما أن الشيء إذا كان مملوكا لزيد فهو مملوك لا محالة. وأما صدق الشيء بسيطا دون المركب ومركب دون البسيط فقد مرَّ ذكره من قبل.
When you find something of this kind, look whether it is a part; retain it as a part, add it to what is nearest, make them one term, and discard the other, and convert the proposition to a phrase that does not require you to use that. For if you say 'good exists for wisdom' you need this letter, which is the lam, but if you say 'wisdom has good' you dispense with it; thus you know that the letter lam is not part of the predicate at all. As for when you say 'one knowledge exists in opposites' then say 'opposites have one knowledge', you need the word 'in' or something parallel to it. And it has already been mentioned that such a word appears in both the minor and major premises as indicating the part, as in your saying 'every quality has belief', and 'everything that has belief has a subject'; so every quality has a subject. Sometimes the matter differs in the two premises, so the part-indicator is in the major, and the other in the minor, as in your saying 'knowledge exists in every quality', and 'quality exists in every whiteness'. Sometimes it is the reverse, as in your saying 'good exists in knowledge' and 'knowledge exists in every quality'. And this is not only in affirmative but also in negative, as in your saying 'not in the universe is universe', and 'not to motion is motion'. For if each of the two letters indicates predication, each of the two propositions is false; but if each of them is part of the predicate, they are true. For if you say 'nothing of the universe exists for the universe', and 'the universe exists in every pleasure', you have taken the lam in the major as a part, resulting in that pleasure has no universe, and that is true; and every pleasure is a universe, and the universe is not universe. And if you take it as indicating predication, your statement that the universe does not exist for another is not valid, and it is not predicated on it. Similarly, the sign is not existent for the sign existent for health, and likewise time is existent for Allah, glory be to Him. And these things deliver you from stating the premises in the customary order. For although placing the definitions sometimes requires you to say 'animal is on human' and 'human is on writer', using the premises does not require you to do that; rather you say 'every writer is human, every human is animal'. And know that sometimes a statement is true absolutely, but when a condition is added it becomes false. For it is true that man is sentient, but it is not true that he is sentient-soul. And sometimes it is false absolutely, as in your saying 'man is non-existent'; if it is said 'non-existent in counterpart', it is true. As for what is not like that, it is true absolutely and conditionally, just as when something is owned by Zayd, it is owned without doubt. As for the truth of a thing simply without composition, or composite without simple, its mention has preceded.
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on prepositions in propositions. The author advises: when you find a preposition, see if it is part of the predicate; if so, retain it as part, add it to the nearest, and make them one term, dropping the other, and rephrase the proposition to avoid the preposition if possible. He gives an example: 'good exists for wisdom'—you need the 'lam' if you keep the phrase, but if you rephrase to 'wisdom has good', you avoid it, showing the lam is not part of the predicate. Then he discusses cases where the preposition is part of the predicate in both premises, as in 'every quality has belief' and 'everything that has belief has a subject'—here 'fi' is part of the predicate. Sometimes the preposition differs between premises, e.g. 'knowledge exists in every quality' and 'quality exists in every whiteness'—the part-indicator is in the major, and the other in the minor. He also covers negatives: 'not in the universe is universe' and 'not to motion is motion'. Next, he examines truth conditions: if the preposition indicates predication (haml), the proposition is false; if it is part of the predicate, it is true. For example, 'nothing of the universe exists for the universe'—if 'lam' is taken as part of the predicate, the conclusion 'pleasure has no universe' is true; if taken as predication, it fails. The author also discusses ordering premises: instead of 'animal is on human' and 'human is on writer', use 'every writer is human, every human is animal' by conversion. Finally, he discusses absolute vs. conditional statements: sometimes a statement is true absolutely but false when conditioned, e.g. 'man is sentient' true, but 'man is sentient soul' false; and vice versa: 'man is non-existent' false absolutely, but 'man is non-existent in counterpart' true; some statements are true both simply and conditionally, like 'something owned by Zayd is owned'.

Key Terms
قلب القضية (qalb al-qadiyya): Conversion of a proposition; changing a proposition to a logically equivalent form, here to remove a preposition.
عكس (aks): Conversion (in categorical logic); swapping subject and predicate while preserving truth conditions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the converted form of 'الحيوان على الإنسان'?
A) كل إنسان حيوان
B) كل حيوان إنسان
C) الحيوان هو الإنسان
D) الإنسان على الحيوان

Key Takeaway
Propositions with non-predicate prepositions can be rephrased to standard forms, and premises can be reordered by conversion to achieve a clean syllogism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Convert the proposition 'ليس للحركة حركة' to a form without preposition, using the author's method. Then state the converted form.
?
Take the two premises 'الحيوان على الإنسان' and 'الإنسان على الكاتب'. Convert each to standard categorical form (e.g., 'كل إنسان حيوان', 'كل كاتب إنسان') and then write the resulting syllogism.

Absolute vs. Conditional Truth of Propositions
Author explains the difference between absolute (mursal) truth and conditional truth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify propositions as true absolutely, true only conditionally, or false absolutely, based on the author's examples and criteria.

Core Idea
A proposition may be true when stated simply but false when conditioned, or vice versa.

The author introduces an important nuance: not all propositions behave the same way when a condition is added. Some are true absolutely (mursal) but become false when a specific condition is added; others are false absolutely but become true with a condition. This lesson will help you distinguish these cases and understand their implications for logical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify a proposition stated absolutely (mursal)
Look for a proposition without any condition. For example, 'al-insan hassaas' (man is sentient) is stated absolutely.
2
Add a condition and observe truth change
The author says that 'man is sentient' is true absolutely, but when you add the condition 'sentient-soul' (hassaas an-nafs), it becomes false. This is because 'sentient-soul' implies something more than mere sentience.
The condition restricts or extends the predicate.
3
Identify a proposition false absolutely that becomes true with condition
Consider 'al-insan ma'dum' (man is non-existent). This is false when absolute. But if you add the condition 'non-existent in counterpart' (ma'dum an-nadhir), it becomes true. The condition clarifies the context.
The condition makes the proposition true by specifying the respect.
4
Distinguish propositions true both absolutely and conditionally
Some propositions remain true regardless. Example: 'ash-shay' idha kana mamlukan li-Zayd fahuwa mamluk' (if something is owned by Zayd, it is owned). This is true absolutely and conditionally.
These are logically robust statements.

Commentary

Key Terms
مرسل (mursal): Absolute/unconditional statement; a proposition without any qualifying condition.
شرط (shart): Condition; an added qualification that restricts or extends the proposition's scope.

Subject and Predicate in Syllogistic Premises
Author explains the subject of every proposition and the role of the middle term in premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the subject and predicate in categorical syllogism premises and distinguish between the indicator of predication and the part of the predicate.

Core Idea
Every proposition has a subject; the middle term's role changes with premise order.

This passage delves into the structure of categorical propositions within syllogisms. The author clarifies how to identify the subject of a proposition and shows how the same term can function as subject in one premise and predicate in another. Crucially, he distinguishes between terms that merely indicate predication and those that form part of the predicate content, a distinction that affects truth value. Understanding this is essential for correctly ordering premises and constructing valid syllogisms in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject of a proposition
Every proposition that conveys assent (tasdiq) has a subject. Look for the entity about which something is asserted. For example, in 'Knowledge exists in every quality,' the subject is 'quality' because it is what knowledge is asserted of.
2
Determine the middle term's position
In a syllogism, the middle term appears in both premises but in different roles. In 'Knowledge exists in every quality, and quality exists in every whiteness,' the term 'quality' is predicate in the first premise and subject in the second. This is the typical pattern for a valid first-figure syllogism.
The author contrasts this with the reverse order, as in 'Good exists in knowledge, and knowledge exists in every quality.'
3
Distinguish indicator of predication from part of predicate
A term can serve as an indicator of predication (dal 'ala al-haml), simply showing the relation, or as part of the predicate. In 'There is no existence in existence,' if 'existence' is only an indicator, the proposition is false; if it is part of the predicate, it is true. The author demonstrates this with the Arabic letter 'lam' (ل), which can be a preposition (part of predicate) or a predication marker.
This distinction is vital for evaluating truth in negative propositions.
4
Apply to negative propositions
The same structure applies to negatives. In 'There is no motion for motion,' the term 'motion' can be interpreted as part of the predicate or as a predication indicator, affecting truth.
5
Order premises correctly
Natural order might place terms like 'Animal upon human,' but in syllogistic form we order them as 'Every writer is human, and every human is writer.' This ensures the middle term mediates properly and the conclusion follows.
The author emphasizes that premise order matters for validity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكل ما فيه تصديق فله موضوع؛ فكل كيفية له موضوع وربَّما اختلف الأمر في المقدمتين فكان الدال على الجزء في الكبرى، والآخر في الصغرى كقولك العلم موجود في كل كيفية، والكيفية موجودة في كل بياض. وربَّما كان بالعكس كقولك الخير موجود في العلم والعلم موجود في كل كيفية؛ وليس هذا في الإيجاب حده بل في السلب أيضًا كقولك ليس في الكون كون، وليس للحركة حركة. فإن كل واحد من الحرفين إذا كان دالا على الحمل كانت كل واحدة من القضيتين كاذبة، وإن كان كل واحد منهما جزءا من المحمول كانت صادقة؛ فإنك إذا قلت لا شيء من الكون موجودًا للكون، والكون موجود في كل لذة فقد أخذتَ اللام في الكبرى جزءًا ينتج أن اللذة ليس لها كون وهو حق، وكل لذة كون، والكون ليس كون. وإذا أخذت دالا على الحمل لم يصح قولك أن الكون ليس موجودا للغير، وليس محمولا عليها. وكذلك العلامة لیست موجودة للعلامة موجودة للصحة وكذلك الوقت موجود الله سبحانه وتعالى؛ وهذه الاشياء تُخلصك قول المقدّمات على الترتيب المستعمل. فإن وضع الحدود وإن كان يحوِّجك أحيانًا إلى أن تقول إن الحيوان على الإنسان، والإنسان على الكاتب؛ فاستعمال المقدمات لا يحوِّجك إليه فتقول كل كاتب إنسان، وكل إنسان كاتب.
And every thing in which there is assent has a subject; so every quality has a subject. And sometimes the matter differs in the two premises, so that the indicator of the part is in the major [premise], and the other in the minor, as when you say 'Knowledge exists in every quality, and quality exists in every whiteness.' And sometimes it is the opposite, as when you say 'Good exists in knowledge, and knowledge exists in every quality.' And this is not only in affirmative [propositions] but also in negative, as when you say 'There is no existence in existence, and there is no motion for motion.' For if each of the two terms is an indicator of predication, then each of the two propositions is false; but if each of them is part of the predicate, then they are true. For if you say 'Nothing of existence exists for existence, and existence exists in every pleasure,' you have taken the lam in the major as a part, resulting that pleasure has no existence, which is true; and 'Every pleasure is existence, and existence is not existence.' And if you take it as an indicator of predication, it is not correct for you to say that existence does not exist for another, nor is it predicated. Similarly, 'the marker is not existent for the marker existent for health,' and similarly 'time exists [for] God the Exalted.' And these things lead you to state the premises in the usual order. For placing the limits, although it sometimes necessitates that you say 'Animal upon human, and human upon writer,' the use of premises does not necessitate it, so you say 'Every writer is human, and every human is writer.'
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage defines the structure of categorical propositions in syllogisms. The author states that every proposition conveying assent (تصديق) has a subject (موضوع). He then illustrates how the middle term can appear in different positions in the major and minor premises. In the example 'Knowledge exists in every quality, and quality exists in every whiteness,' the term 'quality' functions as the predicate of the first premise and the subject of the second. The author emphasizes that this applies to negative propositions as well, using the example 'There is no existence in existence.' The key distinction is whether a term acts as an indicator of predication (دال على الحمل) or as part of the predicate (جزء من المحمول). When each term is an indicator of predication, both propositions are false; when each is part of the predicate, they can be true. The author demonstrates this with the Arabic word 'lam' (ل), which can be a preposition (part of predicate) or a predication indicator. Finally, he advises on ordering premises: sometimes it is natural to say 'Animal upon human,' but in syllogistic form we say 'Every writer is human.' This clarifies how to properly structure arguments in the Islamic logical tradition, where examples like 'Every writer is human' are used instead of secular ones.

Key Terms
تصديق
دال على الحمل
وضع الحدود

Absolute vs Conditional Truth of Statements
Author distinguishes between unconditionally true statements and those true only under a condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between absolute (mursal) and conditional (shart) truth of statements and identify examples of each from Islamic logic.

Core Idea
A statement's truth can change depending on whether a condition is added or removed.

In Islamic logic, truth is not always straightforward. The same statement can be true under one description but false under another if a condition is added or removed. This lesson explores the distinction between absolute truth (truth without any qualification) and conditional truth (truth only when a specific condition is met), as explained by the author. Understanding this distinction is crucial for precise reasoning and avoiding ambiguity in scholarly debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
The passage introduces two categories: statements that are true absolutely (mursal) and statements that are true only under a condition (shart). Note that some statements are false absolutely but true conditionally, and some are true both ways.
Mursal means unrestricted, shart means condition.
2
Analyse the first example
The author says 'The human is sentient' is true absolutely, but 'The human is sentient of the soul' is false. Understand why adding 'of the soul' changes the truth: the soul itself is not sentient in the same way.
This shows that a condition can make a true statement false.
3
Analyse the second example
The statement 'The human is non-existent' is false absolutely, but if we add 'in comparison (to a counterpart)', it becomes true. Reflect on how a condition can rescue a false statement.
This shows the reverse: a false statement can become true conditionally.
4
Understand the third category
Some statements are true both absolutely and conditionally, like 'A thing owned by Zayd is owned.' This is true regardless of any added condition.
The author mentions this to show not all statements change with conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage introduces the distinction between absolute (مرسل) and conditional (بشرط) truth. The author explains that some statements are true unconditionally but become false when a condition is added. For instance, 'The human is sentient' is absolutely true, but 'the human is sentient of the soul' (meaning the soul itself is sentient) is false. Conversely, some statements are false absolutely but can become true under a condition: 'The human is non-existent' is false, but if we add 'in comparison (to a counterpart),' it becomes true. However, some statements are true both absolutely and conditionally, such as 'A thing owned by Zayd is owned.' The author also reminds readers of a previous discussion about simple and compound truth. This classification is important in Islamic logic for understanding how propositions behave in different contexts, particularly in disputation (jadal) where conditions are often specified.

Distinguishing Negative and Privative Propositions
Author distinguishes negative and privative propositions with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a proposition is negative or privative and correctly handle privative terms in categorical syllogisms.

Core Idea
Privative propositions are affirmative with a negated predicate; confusing them with negatives destroys syllogistic validity.

A common error in logic is to mistake a privative proposition (معدولة) for a negative one (سالبة). This confusion can lead to invalid syllogisms and absurd conclusions. In this lesson, we examine the passage where the author clarifies the difference and illustrates how to handle privative terms in syllogisms, using the example 'Every J is not-B; every not-B is A; therefore every J is A.'

Guided Path
1
Recognize the two proposition types
Understand that a negative proposition (سالبة) denies the predicate of the subject (e.g., 'Zayd is not writing'), while a privative proposition (معدولة) affirms a negated predicate (e.g., 'Zayd is not-writing' – affirmative in quality). The negation particle is part of the predicate term in privatives.
Privative propositions are affirmative, but they look like negatives due to the negation particle.
2
Identify the error when premises are privative
The author warns that when one or both premises are privative, people often mistakenly treat them as negatives. This error is especially common when the conclusion is a simple affirmative. Recognize that treating a privative premise as negative changes the logical form and may invalidate the syllogism.
Always check the quality: affimative (معدولة) vs negative (سالبة).
3
Work through the example syllogism
Examine the syllogism given: 'Every J is not-B; whatever is not-B is A; therefore every J is A.' Note that 'not-B' is a privative term. Identify the middle term (not-B), major premise (all not-B are A), minor premise (all J are not-B), and conclusion (all J are A). Confirm that this is a valid first-figure AAA syllogism (Barbara) only if 'not-B' is treated as an affirmative predicate.
If 'not-B' were treated as a negative, the minor premise would become 'No J is B', changing the mood and potentially invalidating the argument.
4
Apply the distinction to avoid absurdities
The author shows that confusing privative with negative leads to contradictions, e.g., incorrectly concluding that the moon is 'wood not-white'. Understand that a privative term like 'not-white' is a positive property (the state of being non-white), not a denial of whiteness. Preserve this distinction to maintain logical consistency.
Privative terms are positive in the sense of having a negated attribute, not negating the attribute itself.

Commentary
This passage addresses a common logical error: mistaking a privative proposition (معدولة) for a negative one (سالبة). The privative is actually an affirmative proposition where the negation particle is part of the predicate term, e.g., 'Zayd is not-writing' means Zayd has the privative property of not-writing, which is affirmative. The author then discusses the position of the negation particle and gives examples of contradiction: 'existing not-white' contradicts 'existing white', and 'wood white' contradicts 'wood not-white'. He then constructs a detailed example with four terms: A (composed), B (not composed, contradictory of A), C (white), D (not white, contradictory of C). He shows that A is more general than C, so D is more general than B. He warns that confusing privative with negative leads to absurdities, such as incorrectly concluding that the moon is wood not-white (an absurd example). The passage ends with a note on truth conditions: when A is false of C, D is true of C, etc.

Distinguishing Negative and Privatized Propositions in Syllogisms
Author explains the confusion between negative and privatized propositions and provides the rule to avoid errors.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a negative (saliba) and a privatized (ma'dula) proposition in syllogistic contexts and correctly treat privatized propositions as affirmative.

Core Idea
A privatized proposition is affirmative; its negation is part of the predicate term.

In syllogistic reasoning, confusing privatized (ma'dula) propositions with negative (saliba) ones is a common error that makes it difficult to reduce arguments to the figures. The author provides a clear rule: treat the privatized proposition as an affirmative, considering the negative particle as part of the predicate term. This lesson will help you parse and apply this distinction using the examples given.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two proposition types
A negative proposition (saliba) denies the predicate of the subject (e.g., 'Zayd is not walking'). A privatized proposition (ma'dula) has a predicate that includes a negation (e.g., 'Zayd is not-walking'), forming a single term. The former denies the relation; the latter affirms a negative term.
2
Locate the negation's position
In a negative, the negation applies to the copula (the 'is' or 'huwa'). In a privatized, the negation is part of the predicate term. In Arabic, look for the negative particle after 'huwa' or the existential word to indicate a negative, versus before or as part of the predicate for a privatized.
The author says there is no difference whether the negation occurs after or before the copula in terms of the rule—what matters is whether it is part of the term.
3
Apply the rule to the syllogism example
Consider the example: 'Every J is not-B' and 'What is not-B is A'. If 'not-B' is a privatized term, then the first premise is an affirmative privatized, not a negative. Treat it as affirmative: 'Every J is (not-B)'. Then the syllogism yields 'Every J is A' correctly. Mistaking it for a negative would affect validity.
The author warns that most errors occur when the conclusion is a simple affirmative.
4
Practice with the contrary examples
The author gives opposition pairs: 'existing not-white' is contrary to 'existing white', and 'wood white' is contrary to 'wood not-white'. Recognize that these are privatized terms; 'wood not-white' is a positive predicate that includes negation. Use these to reinforce the distinction.
Everything is either wood white or wood not-white—so 'the moon is wood not-white' is a privatized affirmative, not a negative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشركة فيها ومما تعذر معه أمر الحل إلى الأشكال اشتباه السالبة والمعدولة فإنه إذا كانت المقدِّمتان أو إحديهما معدولة يظن أنها سالبة؛ وأكثر هذا الغلط فيما إذا كانت النتيجة موجبة بسيطة كما في قولنا كل «ج» هو لا «ب» وما هو لا «ب» فهو «أ»، فكل ج (أ) فيجب أن تراعى حال السلب والعدول، وأن تأخذ المعدول موجبًا وحرف السلب جزءًا من الحد الذي يقرن به وخصوصا من المحمول. والفرق بين الموجبة المعدولة والسالبة والحال في تلازمها ولا تلازمها قد تقدم فلا حاجة إلى البيان في هذا الموضع ليتبين به الفرق بين أن يرد السلب بعد كلمة «هو» وبعد الكلمة الوجودية، وبين أن يرد قبلهما بل لا فرق بين أن يرد بعدهما وبين أن يرد بعد معنى آخر.

The confusion between the negative and the privatized is common, and with it the matter of solving into the figures becomes difficult. For if the two premises or one of them is privatized, it is thought to be negative; most of this error occurs when the conclusion is a simple affirmative, as in our saying 'Every J is not-B' and 'What is not-B is A', so every J is A. So one must consider the state of negation and privation, and take the privatized as affirmative and the letter of negation as part of the term with which it is combined, especially of the predicate. The difference between the affirmative privatized and the negative, and the state of their mutual entailment or non-entailment, has preceded, so there is no need for clarification in this place to distinguish between the negation occurring after the word 'huwa' and after the existential word, and occurring before them; rather there is no difference between its occurrence after them and its occurrence after another meaning. For if our saying 'existing not-white' is contrary to our saying 'existing white', then our saying 'it can walk' is contrary to our saying 'it can not walk'; rather our saying 'wood white' is contrary to 'wood not-white'; so everything is either wood white or wood not-white, so the moon is wood and not-white. Source pages p. 309

Commentary
This passage is a clarification of a common error in syllogistic reasoning: confusing privatized (ma'dula) propositions with negative (saliba) propositions. The author begins by stating that this confusion makes it difficult to reduce arguments to the syllogistic figures. The error occurs especially when the conclusion is a simple affirmative, as in the example: 'Every J is not-B' (a premiss that looks negative but is actually a positive privatized, with 'not-B' as a single term) and 'What is not-B is A' yields 'Every J is A'. The rule is to treat the privatized proposition as an affirmative and to consider the negative particle as part of the predicate term. The author then notes that the distinction between affirmative privatized and negative has been explained earlier, so he does not repeat it. He then gives examples of opposition: 'existing not-white' is contrary to 'existing white', similarly 'can walk' is contrary to 'can not walk'. He illustrates with 'wood white' and 'wood not-white', explaining that everything is either one or the other, so the moon is 'wood not-white' (a privative term). The passage concludes by warning that if one mistakenly treats a privatized as equivalent to a negative, absurdity results.

Key Terms
سالبة (saliba): A negative proposition, where the negation has wide scope over the predicate, e.g., 'Zayd is not walking'.
معدولة (ma'dula): A privatized proposition, where the negative particle is part of the predicate term, forming a privative term, e.g., 'Zayd is not-walking'.
موجبة معدولة (mujiba ma'dula): An affirmative proposition with a privated predicate, considered as affirmative because the negation is part of the term.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Treat privatized propositions as affirmative in syllogistic reasoning because their negation is part of the predicate term, not a denial of the predicative relation.

True Premises Imply True Conclusion (But Not Vice Versa)
Author explains asymmetry between truth of premises and conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a true conclusion does not guarantee true premises, using examples from Islamic logic.

Core Idea
Truth flows forward in a valid syllogism, but a true conclusion can hide false premises.

In this passage, the author establishes a fundamental asymmetry in logic: true premises always yield a true conclusion in a valid syllogism, but the converse does not hold. This is crucial for evaluating arguments in Islamic sciences, where one might encounter a true conclusion derived from unsound premises. The author illustrates with concrete examples using terms like 'human', 'stone', and 'animal'.

Guided Path
1
Understand the principle
The author states: true premises (with correct syllogistic form) necessarily yield a true conclusion. Notice that the conclusion is a logical consequent, and truth flows forward.
2
See the converse failure
The author emphasizes that the converse does not hold: a true conclusion does not imply the premises are true. This is because false premises can coincidentally lead to a true conclusion if the form is valid.
3
Analyze the first example (second figure)
The author uses variables J, B, A. With true premises 'Every J is B, No A is B', conclusion 'No J is A' is true. Swapping to 'No J is B, Every A is B' makes premises false but conclusion still follows. Identify that the truth of the conclusion is independent of the truth of the premises in this case.
4
Analyze the second example (first figure)
The author uses concrete terms: 'Every human is a stone, every stone is an animal' yields 'Every human is an animal'. Check that the premises are false (humans are not stones), but the conclusion is true. The form (Barbara) is valid.
5
Reflect on the definition of syllogism
The author reminds that a syllogism is defined by formal entailment, not by truth of premises. So a syllogism can be valid with false premises.

فصل : [ في أن المقدّمات الصادقة تلزمها النتيجة الصادقة ولا ينعكس] واعلم بأن المقدمات الصادقة تلزمها النتيجة الصادقة؛ ولا ينعكس فإن القياس نحو المقدم للنتيجة والنتيجة كالتالي ولا يلزم من رفع المقدم وهو القياس المشتمل على صدق المقدمات وصواب التأليف رفع التالي وهو النتيجة الصادقة لكن يلزم من وضعه وضع التالي على ما عرف. ولما كانت الحالة هذه فلا يمنع أن تلزم من المقدمات الكاذبة نتيجة صادقة لا لأن صدقها أو حقيقة تلك المقدّمات بل لأنه اتفق أن كانت صادقة لذاتها لكن انتظم على لزومها القياس المركَّب من تلك المقدمات الكاذبة. ومثال ذلك أنك إذا قلت كل ج ب ، ولا شيء من «أب» على أنهما صادقتان ينتج من الشكل الثاني لا شيء من (ج) أ»؛ ثم إذا جعلت الموجبة منهما سالبة والسالبة موجبة من غير تغير الحدود، وقلت لا شيء من (ج) ب ، وكل «أب» كانت كل واحدة منهما كاذبة حينئذ. ومع ذلك فالمؤلف منهما ينتج لا شيء من «ج أ». وكذلك في الموجبة كما إذا أخذ شيئان متباينان، وحمل جنس أحدهما على الآخر ثم حمل ذلك على المباين الآخر ينتج أن يحمل المباين على المباين كما إذا قلت كل إنسان حجر ، وكل حجر حيوان ينتج كل إنسان حيوان ولو قلت في الكبرى ولا شيء من الحجر بجماد ينتج لا شيء من الإنسان بجماد ولا يظن أنه ليس بقياس؛ والقياس هو قول مؤلف من أقوال متى سلمت لزم عنه لذاته قول آخر، وإنه كذلك. [1] وأما كيفية إنتاج هذا القياس في الشكل الأول فنقول الكذب إما أن يكون في الكبرى فقط، أو في الصغرى فقط، أو فيهما جميعا. وعلى كل واحد من التقادير فالمطلوب إما أن يكون كليا وجزئيّاً ؛ فهذه ستة أقسام: [١] الأول أن
Section: On that true premises imply a true conclusion, and it does not reverse. Know that true premises imply a true conclusion, and it does not reverse. For the syllogism is like the antecedent to the consequent, and the conclusion is like the consequent. It is not necessary that from raising the antecedent—which is the syllogism comprising the truth of premises and correctness of composition—the raising of the consequent, which is the true conclusion, follows; rather, from positing it follows the positing of the consequent, as is known. Since this is the case, it is not prevented that from false premises a true conclusion follows—not because of the truth or reality of those premises, but because it happens that it is true in itself, but the syllogism composed of those false premises is arranged to entail it. An example of that is when you say 'Every J is B, and no A is B' assuming they are true, then from the second figure it produces 'No J is A'. Then if you make the affirmative of them negative and the negative affirmative without changing the terms, and say 'No J is B, and every A is B', then each of them is false at that time, yet the composition from them produces 'No J is A'. Likewise in the affirmative, as when you take two mutually exclusive things, and predicate the genus of one on the other, then predicate that on the other exclusive, it results in predicating the exclusive on the exclusive, as when you say 'Every human is a stone, and every stone is an animal' it produces 'Every human is an animal'. And if you say in the major 'No stone is an inanimate' it produces 'No human is an inanimate'. Do not think that it is not a syllogism; the syllogism is a statement composed of statements such that when they are accepted, another statement follows from them essentially, and it is so. [1] As for how this syllogism produces in the first figure, we say: falsehood is either in the major only, or in the minor only, or in both. And for each of these cases, the sought conclusion is either universal or particular; these are six divisions: [1] the first is that...
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage opens a new section on the logical relationship between truth of premises and truth of conclusion. The author states a key principle: true premises necessarily yield a true conclusion, but the converse does not hold—a true conclusion does not imply that the premises are true. He frames this in terms of antecedent and consequent: the syllogism with true premises and proper form is the antecedent, and the true conclusion is the consequent; positing the antecedent entails positing the consequent, but denying the antecedent does not entail denying the consequent. Then he explains that false premises can coincidentally lead to a true conclusion, not because of the premises' truth but because the conclusion is true in itself and the syllogistic form correctly entails it. He provides two examples using categorical syllogisms. In the first, he uses terms J, B, A in the second figure: with true premises 'Every J is B, No A is B', the conclusion 'No J is A' is true. If we swap the qualities to 'No J is B, Every A is B', the premises become false, yet the same conclusion follows. In the second example, he uses the terms 'insan' (human), 'hajar' (stone), 'hayawan' (animal), and 'jamad' (inanimate) to show that from false premises 'Every human is a stone, Every stone is an animal' the true conclusion 'Every human is an animal' follows. He then reminds the definition of syllogism. Finally, he begins a classification of cases based on which premise is false and whether the conclusion is universal or particular, enumerating six divisions (but the text cuts off at the first). The passage underscores that logical validity does not guarantee material truth of premises; rather, it preserves truth forward.

النتيجة الصادقة
الكذب

Key Takeaway
A valid syllogism preserves truth from premises to conclusion, but a true conclusion does not guarantee true premises.

Potential Knowledge from Universal Affirmative Propositions
Author explains how assent yields potential knowledge of related propositions, and analyzes the universal affirmative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the five relations of potential knowledge from a universal affirmative proposition and determine what can and cannot be derived from 'كل ب أ'.

Core Idea
Assenting to 'Every B is A' gives potential knowledge of its particulars, but not of all universals containing it.

When you know that every B is A actually, you also gain potential knowledge of related propositions: the necessary condition, the opposite, the particular instances, and the universal categories. In this lesson, we dissect the meaning of the universal affirmative 'كل ب أ' and precisely map what can be derived from it potentially and what cannot, using the tools from this passage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five relations of potential knowledge
The passage lists five things that a known proposition gives potential knowledge of: its necessary condition (malzum), its opposite (mu'anid), a universal above it, a particular under it, or something together with it. List them and note the Arabic terms.
The relations are: ملزوم, معاند, كلي فوقه, جزئي تحته, or معه.
2
Understand the direction of derivation for each relation
For each relation, note the mechanism: from malzum to lazim (entailment), from mu'anid to opposition, from universal to particular (syllogism), from particular to universal (induction/analogy). Recognize that potential knowledge is directional.
The direction is important: knowing the universal gives potential knowledge of the particular, but not vice versa directly without induction.
3
Parse the meaning of 'كل ب أ'
The author defines it as 'كل فرد يجب أن يكون ب فهو أ'. Break it down: كل (every), ب (subject term), أ (predicate). The inclusion of 'يجب' (must) indicates necessity. So the proposition means 'Every individual that must be B is A.' This is not simply 'All B are A' but implies a necessary connection.
The necessity marker 'يجب' is important for understanding the epistemic strength.
4
Distinguish actual from potential knowledge
The author says that knowing 'كل ب أ' gives actual knowledge that everything described by B is A. But this does not yield potential knowledge that A is predicated of some other term J (e.g., if you know that every J is A). The potential knowledge is only in the specific relations listed, not arbitrarily transitive.
Example: knowing every B is A does not give potential knowledge that every J is A, even if J is a universal containing B.
5
Check the priority of knowledge
The passage concludes that prior knowledge is by essence (بالذات), not necessarily by time. This means that in seeking knowledge, the first thing sought is the essential prior, not temporally prior. Apply this to the example: in a syllogism, the premises are prior by essence to the conclusion.
This has implications for the order of learning and demonstration in Islamic sciences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والشيء إذا وقع التصديق به لكان تصديقا بالقوة لشيء آخر فهو إما ملزومه وإما معانده أو كلي فوقه أو جزئي تحته أو معه. والملزوم إذا علم بالفعل كان ذلك العلم علمًا بالقوة إلى لازمه. وعلى هذا إذا علم المعاند بالفعل كان ذلك العلم علما بمعاندة إما بالوضع وإما برفعه والكلي إذا علم وجود حكم عليه كان ذلك علما بالقوة بالجزئي الذي تحته بطريق القياس والجزئي إذا عُلم وجود حكم عليه كان ذلك كليًّا بالقوة بالكلي الذي فوقه على ما عرف في القياس والاستقراء والتمثيل. فإذن كل صنف من العلم والظن المكتسب إذا كان اكتسابه ذهنيًا فهو علم أو ظن سابق ثمَّ إذا قلنا كل ب أ فمعناه كل واحد مما يجب (ب) فهو أ، فهذه المعرفة بالقوة التي كأنها فعل والعلم بأن الأوسط موجود للأصغر ليس علما بالقوة بأن الأكبر موجود له؛ فإن كون الأكبر للأصغر ليس مندرجا في كون الأصغر للأوسط بل الأمر بالعكس فإنك إذا علمت أن كل ب أ فقد علمت أن كل ما يوصف بـ ب فهو أ. أما إذا علمت أن كل ج أ فلم يدخل أ الذي هو محمول على ب؛ وهذا لا بفعل ولا بقوة. ثمَّ العلم السابق كما مرَّ أنه سابق بالزمان عند بعضهم؛ والحق أنه سابق بالذات، وإليه يوجه أول الطلب بالذات.
When assent occurs to something, it becomes potential assent to something else: either its necessary condition (malzum), its opposite (mu'ānid), a universal above it, a particular under it, or together with it. If the necessary condition is known actually, that knowledge is potential knowledge of its necessary consequent. Similarly, if the opposite is known actually, that knowledge is knowledge of opposition either by position or by removal. The universal, if the existence of a ruling on it is known, that knowledge is potential knowledge of the particular under it by way of syllogism. The particular, if the existence of a ruling on it is known, that is potential knowledge of the universal above it, as known in syllogism, induction, and analogy. So every species of acquired knowledge or opinion, if its acquisition is mental, it is prior knowledge or opinion. Then when we say 'Every B is A', its meaning is: every individual that must be B is A. This is potential knowledge that is almost actual. The knowledge that the middle term exists for the minor term is not potential knowledge that the major term exists for it; for the existence of the major for the minor is not contained in the existence of the minor for the middle term, but rather the opposite. For if you know that every B is A, you know that everything described by B is A. But if you know that every J is A, then A which is predicated of B is not included; this is neither actual nor potential. Then prior knowledge, as previously, is prior in time according to some; but the truth is that it is prior by essence, and to it the first seeking is directed by essence.
Source pages p. 369

Commentary
This passage explores the epistemological consequences of assent. When we assent to a proposition, that assent gives us potential knowledge of related propositions: the malzum (necessary condition) gives potential knowledge of its necessary consequent; knowing the opposite gives knowledge of opposition; knowing a universal gives potential knowledge of its particulars via syllogism; knowing a particular gives potential knowledge of its universal through induction or analogy. The author then analyzes the universal affirmative proposition 'كل ب أ' (Every B is A), explaining that it means every individual that must be B is A. He emphasizes that knowing that the middle term applies to the minor term does not automatically give potential knowledge that the major applies to the minor; rather, the major's applicability to the minor is not contained in the minor's applicability to the middle. Thus, potential knowledge is not simply transitive. Finally, the author addresses the nature of priority: some say prior in time, but the truth is priority by essence (bi al-dhāt).

Key Terms

لازم
معاند
بالقوة
بالفعل
كل ب أ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Every B is A, with no further qualification.
B) Every individual that must be B is A.
C) Some B is A in potentiality.
D) Every A is B.

Key Takeaway
The universal affirmative 'كل ب أ' yields potential knowledge of its particulars via syllogism and of its necessary conditions and opposites, but not indiscriminately; the priority of knowledge is essential, not temporal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Types of Accepted Premises (Taslim)
Classification of premises by acceptance type.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the types of accepted premises (taslim) and determine whether a given premise is correct or incorrect acceptance.

Core Idea
Accepted premises rely on authority or habit, not certainty.

In the previous lesson, we learned that syllogisms are built on premises, which may be necessary or accepted. This lesson focuses on accepted premises (taslim), which are not certain but are used in arguments based on authority, convention, or consensus. The author classifies them to help us evaluate their reliability.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish correct from incorrect acceptance
The passage first divides taslim into correct (sawab) and incorrect (ghayr sawab). Correct acceptance is further divided.
2
Identify shared vs specific acceptance
Correct taslim is either shared (mushtarak fih) or specific (khas). Shared acceptance is based on a group's opinion or divergently accepted by all.
3
Characterize shared acceptance
Shared acceptance can be based on a specific group (like a school of thought) or be widespread but not necessary. People are habituated to it and do not doubt it.
4
Understand the condition of habituated acceptance
The author explains that if one reflects with pure reason, removing custom and training, such premises would not be certain. They involve a middle term but not necessity.
This highlights that habituated acceptance is psychological, not logical.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما ما يكون على سبيل التسليم فإما أن يكون على سبيل تسليم صواب، وإما أن يكون على سبيل تسليم غير صواب. والأول إما على سبيل تسليم مشترك فيه، وإما على سبيل تسليم خاص يكون نافعًا في القياس الذي يخاطب به الواحد الخاص. والذي على سبيل تسليم مشترك فيه إما أن يكون رأيًا يستند إلى طائفة، أو لا يكون بل يكون متفارقًا قبوله في الناس كلهم . وقد مرَّنوا عليه فهم فلا يحلونه محل الشك. وإن كان معه ما إذا اعتبره المميز فعل نفسه كأنه حصل في العالم دفعة. وإنه لم يعود شيئًا ولم يؤدَّب ولم يلتفت إلى حاكم غير العقل ولم ينفعل عن الحياء والخجل فيكون حكمه خلقيا لا عقليًا، ولم ينظر إلى موجب مصلحة فيكون بوسط لا لضرورة. ولم يلتفت إلى الاستقراء أيضًا فإنه بوسط كذلك، ولا إلى أنه هل ينتقض لشيء. وإذا فعل هذا كله ورام
As for what is by way of acceptance, it is either by way of correct acceptance or by way of incorrect acceptance. The first is either shared acceptance or specific acceptance that is useful in a syllogism addressed to a particular individual. That which is shared acceptance is either an opinion based on a group, or not but is accepted in a divergent way among all people. They have been habituated to it so they do not place it in the position of doubt. Even if along with it there is what, if a discerning person considered it, he would [realize] that it occurred suddenly in the world, and he had not become accustomed to anything, and was not trained, and did not pay attention to a judge other than reason, and was not affected by shame and modesty, so his judgment would be natural not rational, and did not consider a motive of benefit, so it would be by a middle term not by necessity, and did not consider induction either, for it is also by a middle term, nor whether it is contradicted by something. And if he does all this and intends...
Source pages p. 371

Commentary
This passage continues the classification of the principles of syllogisms, focusing on premises that are accepted (taslim) rather than necessary. The author divides taslim into correct (sawab) and incorrect (ghayr sawab). The correct type is further divided into shared acceptance (mushtarak fih) — which is either based on the opinion of a specific group or divergently accepted by all people — and specific acceptance (khas) useful in addressing a particular individual. The author then describes the nature of habituated acceptance: people are accustomed to it and do not doubt it, but if one were to reflect with pure reason without custom or training, the premise would not be regarded as certain. Such acceptance may be based on custom, not reason, and involves a middle term (i.e., reasoning) but not necessity. The passage is cut off, but it likely continues with further conditions.

Classification of Premises: Mashhoorat and Awwaliyat
Author classifies premises based on type of acceptance, focusing on mashhoorat and awwaliyat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between mashhoorat, awwaliyat, and maqboolat based on their mode of acceptance, and identify examples from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Common acceptance does not guarantee truth; primary premises are a subset of commonly accepted ones.

In this passage, the author breaks down premises according to how they are accepted—a key skill for evaluating arguments. You will learn to tell apart those that are universally accepted (mashhoorat), those that are self-evident (awwaliyat), and those accepted on specific authority (maqboolat). This classification is vital for assessing the strength of premises in Islamic theological and legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main division
The passage splits acceptance into correct and incorrect, then further divisions. Focus on correct acceptance and trace its branches.
2
Trace the branches
Follow the tree: correct acceptance -> shared vs specific; shared -> based on group (common to all) or based on group (mashhoorat mahmooda) or trusted individuals (maqboolat).
3
Understand the relationship with awwaliyat
Note the final statement: all awwaliyat are mashhoorat, but not all mashhoorat are awwaliyat. This is a logical subalternation.
4
Reflect on the epistemic status
The author says mashhoorat may be false or need proof to become certain, unlike awwaliyat which are self-evident.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما ما يكون على سبيل التسليم فإما أن يكون على سبيل تسليم صواب، وإما أن يكون على سبيل تسليم غير صواب. والأول إما على سبيل تسليم مشترك فيه، وإما على سبيل تسليم خاص يكون نافعًا في القياس الذي يخاطب به الواحد الخاص. والذي على سبيل تسليم مشترك فيه إما أن يكون رأيًا يستند إلى طائفة، أو لا يكون بل يكون متفارقًا قبوله في الناس كلهم. وقد مرَّنوا عليه فهم فلا يحلونه محل الشك. وإن كان معه ما إذا اعتبره المميز فعل نفسه كأنه حصل في العالم دفعة. وإنه لم يعود شيئًا ولم يؤدَّب ولم يلتفت إلى حاكم غير العقل ولم ينفعل عن الحياء والخجل فيكون حكمه خلقيا لا عقليًا، ولم ينظر إلى موجب مصلحة فيكون بوسط لا لضرورة. ولم يلتفت إلى الاستقراء أيضًا فإنه بوسط كذلك، ولا إلى أنه هل ينتقض لشيء. وإذا فعل هذا كله ورام أن يحله محل الشك أمكنه الشك كقولهم إن العدل جميل والظلم قبيح، وإن شكر المنعم واجب فإن هذه مشهورات مقبولة . فإن كانت صادقة فصدقها ليس مما تبين بفطرة العقل بل هي وأمثالها منها ما هو صادق ولكن يحتاج في أن يصير يقينا إلى حجة. ومنها ما هو صادق بشرط دقيق لا يتفطن له الجمهور. ولا يبعد أن يكون في المشهورات كاذب، فهذه هي المشهورات المطلقة. وأما الذي يستند إلى طائفة فمثل ما يستند إلى أمة أو إلى أرباب صناعة؛ ويسمى مشهورات محمودة، ومثل ما يستند إلى واحد أو اثنين أو عدد معين يوثق به؛ ويخص باسم المقبولات. ثم الأوليات بجميعها مشهورة؛ ولا ينعكس.
As for what is by way of acceptance, it is either acceptance of something correct, or acceptance of something incorrect. The first is either shared acceptance, or specific acceptance that is useful in reasoning addressed to a particular individual. That which is shared acceptance is either an opinion based on a group, or it is not, but rather its acceptance is spread among all people. They have practiced it and thus do not consider it doubtful. And if there is with it that which, if the discerning person considers it, he acts as if it occurred in the world suddenly, and he had not returned to anything, nor was he trained, nor did he heed a ruler other than reason, nor was he affected by shyness or shame, so his judgment would be moral not rational, nor did he consider a beneficial cause, so it is by a medium not by necessity. Nor did he consider induction, for it is also by a medium, nor did he consider whether it is contradicted by something. If he does all this and aims to make it doubtful, he can doubt it, like their saying: 'Justice is good, injustice is bad,' and 'Thanking the benefactor is obligatory.' For these are commonly accepted (mashhoorat). If they are true, their truth is not made evident by the innate intellect, but they and their like are among those which are true but need a proof to become certain. And among them are those which are true under a subtle condition not noticed by the public. And it is not unlikely that among the commonly accepted there are false ones. These are the absolute commonly accepted (mashhoorat mutlaqa). As for that which is based on a group, such as what is based on a nation or on the people of a craft; it is called 'praiseworthy commonly accepted' (mashhoorat mahmooda). And such as what is based on one or two or a specific number who are trusted; it is specifically named 'accepted' (maqboolat). Then all primary premises (awwaliyat) are commonly accepted; but the converse does not hold.
Source pages p. 371

Commentary
This passage defines the different types of premises based on how they are accepted. The author begins by distinguishing between acceptance of something correct and incorrect. For correct acceptance, further division into shared (musharak) and specific. Shared acceptance is either based on a group opinion (like a religious sect or profession) or is common to all people. The latter are called mashhoorat (commonly accepted). The author explains that these mashhoorat are so widely accepted that they are not doubted, but a discerning person can still doubt them if he isolates himself from societal influences. Examples given: 'Justice is good, injustice is bad' and 'Thanking the benefactor is obligatory.' These truths are not self-evident to reason; they require demonstration to become certain. Some mashhoorat are true under conditions not noticed by the public, and some may even be false. Then the author mentions group-dependent premises: those based on a nation or craft (called mashhoorat mahmooda) and those based on trusted individuals (called maqboolat). Finally, a note: primary premises (awwaliyat) are all mashhoorat, but not all mashhoorat are awwaliyat. This is a standard classification in Islamic logic.

Key Terms
مشهورات
أوليات
مقبولات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) They are always self-evident.
B) They are all necessarily true.
C) They are accepted by all but may be false.
D) They are only accepted by a specific group.

Key Takeaway
Premises are classified by their mode of acceptance; mashhoorat are commonly accepted but not self-evident, while awwaliyat are both common and self-evident.

Mushabbahat: Sophistical Premises in Logic
Author defines mushabbahat with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify mushabbaha premises by their reliance on ambiguity in wording or meaning and distinguish them from other premise types.

Core Idea
Mushabbahat deceive through verbal or conceptual ambiguity.

In this lesson, we focus on mushabbahat, a type of premise that appears acceptable due to similarity in wording or meaning but is actually used to deceive. The author illustrates with the example of 'Every eye is seeing,' where ambiguity in the word 'ayn' leads to a false conclusion. Understanding mushabbahat is essential for spotting sophistical arguments in theological debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basis of acceptance
The author says mushabbahat are accepted due to similarity (mushabaha) and sharing in wording or meaning. Note that it's an incorrect acceptance—the premise is not actually true but is mistaken for another.
The term 'mushabbahat' literally means 'similarity-based'.
2
Analyze the 'eye' example
The example 'Every eye is seeing' exploits the ambiguity of 'ayn' (eye/spring). The sophist gets the listener to accept that a dinar (coin) is seeing, since a dinar has an 'ayn' (spring/design). The deception lies in the shift of meaning.
This is a classic case of equivocation (fallacy).
3
Analyze the 'intoxicant' example
The premise 'Every intoxicant is khamr' is ambiguous: 'intoxicant' can refer to actual intoxicating effect or potential. The sophist substitutes a potential intoxicant (e.g., unfermented grape juice) to conclude falsely that it is khamr.
The fallacy is a form of amphiboly or ambiguity between potential and actual.
4
Generalize the structure
Both examples show a word or phrase with multiple meanings. The sophist uses one meaning to get acceptance and then switches to another to draw a false conclusion.
This is the defining characteristic of mushabbahat.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المصدق بها على سبيل تسليم غير صواب فهو أن يسلم شيئًا على أنه أمر آخر لمشابهته ومشاركته في اللفظ أو في المعنى على ما يعرف من بعد؛ وهي المقدمات المشبهة كمن يقول كل عين باصرة. ويكون ذلك مسلما له من حيث يفهم منه أحد معاني الاسم المشترك، ويأخذ بدله آخر فيحسّه أنه المعلم أو يقصد به مغالطة حتى يقع في أن يظن بنفسه أو يظن غيره أن الدينار بصير. وكذلك من يسلم أن كل مسكر خمر، ويأخذ بدله ما يكون مسكرًا بالقوة؛ وهذه هي المقدمات المشبهة. وأما المظنونات فهي التي يظن ظنا من غير وقوع اعتقاد جازم؛ وذلك إما لمشابهتها للأمور المشهورة فتكون مشهورة في بادي الرأي الغير المتعتب. فإذا تعقبت عُلم أنها غير مشهورة مثل قولهم انصر أخاك ظالما أو مظلوما»، فإن هذا يظن كما يسمع ثمَّ إذا تعقب كان المشهور أنه لا يجوز أن ينصر
As for that which is accepted by way of incorrect acceptance, it is that one accepts something as another thing due to similarity and sharing in wording or meaning, as will be known later; these are the sophistical premises (mushabbahat), like one saying 'Every eye is seeing.' That is accepted from him because he understands one of the meanings of the ambiguous term, and he substitutes another, feeling that it is the known meaning - or he intends to deceive, so that he or someone else thinks that the dinar (coin) is seeing. Similarly, one who accepts that every intoxicant is khamr, and substitutes what is intoxicant potentially; these are the sophistical premises. As for conjectural premises (maznunat), they are those which are conjectured without a firm belief; that is either due to their similarity to commonly accepted matters, so they are commonly accepted at first glance without reflection. If one reflects, they are known not to be commonly accepted, like their saying 'Support your brother whether he is wronging or wronged,' for this is conjectured as it is heard, then if one reflects, it becomes commonly known that it is not permissible to support...
Source pages p. 372

Commentary
This passage defines two more types of premises: mushabbahat (sophistical) and maznunat (conjectural). Mushabbahat are premises that are accepted due to ambiguity in wording or meaning. The author gives an example: 'Every eye is seeing.' The word 'ayn is ambiguous between 'eye' (organ of sight) and 'spring' (water source). The sophist exploits this to trick someone into thinking a coin (dinar) can see. Another example: 'Every intoxicant is khamr' — here 'intoxicant' could mean actual or potential, so a sophist might substitute a potential intoxicant, leading to a false conclusion. These are used in sophistry (safsata). Then maznunat: premises that are accepted with hesitation, not with certainty. They often resemble mashhoorat at first glance but are not actually commonly accepted. Example: the saying 'Support your brother whether he is wronging or wronged' — at first hearing, it seems plausible, but reflection shows that it is not permissible to support someone who is wronging (i.e., unjust). So such premises are conjectural, not certain.

Key Terms
مشبهات (mushabbahat)
اسم مشترك (ambiguous term)

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) 'The Prophet is truthful' (based on revelation)
D) 'Fire is hot' (observational)

Key Takeaway
Mushabbahat are sophistical premises that rely on verbal or conceptual ambiguity to deceive.

Maznunat: Conjectural Premises in Logic
Author defines maznunat with example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify maznunat premises and explain why they are not reliable despite initial plausibility.

Core Idea
Maznunat only appear true at first glance but fail under scrutiny.

The second type of uncertain premise introduced in this passage is maznunat. These are premises that are conjectured without firm belief. They often resemble commonly accepted premises (mashhoorat) but upon reflection, are found to be invalid. The author gives the example of the saying 'Support your brother whether he is wronging or wronged,' which seems plausible initially but is not actually acceptable. Understanding maznunat helps in evaluating the strength of arguments in dialectical contexts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defining characteristic
The author says maznunat are conjectured (zann) without firm belief (i'tiqad jazim). They are not certain and are often accepted tentatively.
Zann is a technical term meaning 'predominant opinion' without certainty.
2
Recognize the similarity to mashhoorat
Maznunat often resemble mashhoorat (commonly accepted premises) at first glance. This initial plausibility leads to acceptance before reflection.
The difference is that mashhoorat remain acceptable after reflection, while maznunat do not.
3
Analyze the example
The saying 'Support your brother whether he is wronging or wronged' is initially plausible. But after reflection, one realizes that supporting a wrongdoer is not acceptable (it should be to stop the wrongdoing). Thus, it is a maznuna.
The example highlights how a proverb can be misleading without proper context.
4
Contrast with mushabbahat
Unlike mushabbahat (which rely on ambiguity), maznunat are not based on deception but on hasty generalization or superficial appeal. They are merely conjectural.
Both are types of ghayr yaqini premises but differ in cause.

The Fourteen Types of Premises: Overview
Author enumerates and defines fourteen types of premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list all fourteen types of premises from the passage and classify each as certain, common, or imaginary.

Core Idea
Premises vary in certainty: some are self-evident, some are commonly accepted, some are imagined.

The passage divides all premises used in arguments into fourteen types. Understanding this taxonomy is crucial for assessing the epistemic weight of any argument. We will begin by identifying the categories at a high level.

Guided Path
1
Locate the enumeration
Find the part of the passage where the author lists the fourteen principles. It begins with 'فالمبادئ إذن' and ends with 'فهي أربعة عشر صنفًا.' Identify each type by its number.
The numbers may appear as [١] etc.
2
Group by epistemic status
Categorize each type into three groups: certain (e.g., أوليات, محسوسات, مجربات, متواترات, مقدمات فطرية القياسات), common (e.g., مشهورات مطلقة, مشهورات محمودة, مسلمات, مقبولات), and speculative/imaginary (e.g., مخيلات, وهميات, مشبهات, مشهورات في بادئ الرأي, مظنونات).
Some types like المشبهات are fallacious, not speculative.
3
Note key characteristics
For each group, write down one key characteristic mentioned in the passage. For example, certain premises are known by innate reason or experience; common premises are accepted by a community; imaginary premises may become repugnant.
4
Create a mental map
Associate each type with its purpose in argument: demonstration (certain), dialectic (common), rhetoric (praised), sophistry (ambiguous).
This helps recall the taxonomy in practical contexts.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن يحله محل الشك أمكنه الشك كقولهم إن العدل جميل والظلم قبيح، وإن شكر المنعم واجب فإن هذه مشهورات مقبولة . فإن كانت صادقة فصدقها ليس مما تبين بفطرة العقل بل هي وأمثالها منها ما هو صادق ولكن يحتاج في التي يظن ظنا من غير وقوع اعتقاد جازم؛ وذلك إما لمشابهتها للأمور المشهورة فتكون مشهورة في بادي الرأي الغير المتعتب. فإذا تعقبت عُلم أنها غير مشهورة مثل قولهم انصر أخاك ظالما أو مظلوما، فإن هذا يظن كما يسمع ثمَّ إذا تعقب كان المشهور أنه لا يجوز أن ينصر الظالم أخا كان أو ولدا لكنه في الحال يفعل فعله إلى أن يتعقب. وإما أن يقع به الظنُّ على سبيل القبول بغتة، وإما أن يقع الظن بها من جهات أخرى ليس لأحدها على أنها مشهورات كمن يرى فيظنه باطشا به وهذه المظنونات إنما يقع في المقاييس من حيث إن بها اعتقادًا لا من حيث إن مقابلها يختلج في الضمير. فإذن جميع المشهورات وما سلف ذكره معها نافع حيث تنتفع هي لأنها معتقدة فأي صناعة جاز فيها استعمال المظنونات جاز استعمال المذكورات كلها. وكذلك المشهورات إنما ينتفع بها لأنها معتقدة اعتقادًا لا يختلج مقابله فيكون ما قبلها من الأمور الضرورية إذا كانت معتقدة ومسلمة كان نافعًا نفعها فيصلح استعمالها حيث يصلح استعمال تلك. وأما الضروريات الوهمية فإنها بالحري أن تكون أقوى من المسلمات لا في النفع بل في شدة إذعان النفس الغير المقوم لها، فربَّما بقيت مشهورة، وربما صارت شنيعة فتكون كاذبة وشنيعة معا. والسبب في صيرورتها شنيعة ليس ما يدعو إليه من الغرائز والأخلاق والمصالح بل ما يدعو إليه العقل. فالمبادئ إذن [۱] مخيلات [٢] ومحسوسات [٣] ومجربات [٤] ومتواترات [٥] وأوليات [٦] ومقدمات فطرية القياسات [۷] ووهميات [۸] ومشهورات مطلقة [۹] ومشهورات محمودة [١٠] ومسلمات [١١] [٢٧٦] ومقبولات [۱۲] ومشبَّهات [۱۳] ومشهورات في بادئ الرأي الغير المتعتب [١٤] ومظنونات؛ فهي أربعة عشر صنفًا. وهنا قسم من المبادئ وهي التي مبادئ من جهة القايس بغتة كما في المبادئ المذكورة بل من جهة المعلم ؛ وهذه هي الأمور التي تسمى أصولاً موضوعة ومصادرات.
That which can be placed in the place of doubt, doubt is possible concerning it, like their saying: 'Justice is beautiful and injustice is ugly,' and 'Thanking the benefactor is obligatory'—for these are accepted common opinions (mashhoorat). If they are true, their truth is not known by the innate disposition of the intellect, rather they and their likes include some that are true but need a proof to become certain, and some that are true under a precise condition not recognized by the general public. It is not unlikely that falsehood occurs among common opinions. So these are the absolute common opinions. As for that which depends on a group, such as what depends on a nation or the people of a craft, it is called 'praised common opinions'; and like what depends on one or two or a specific number trusted, it is specifically called 'accepted premises' (maqbulat). Then the primary intellective premises (awwaliyat) are all common—but not the converse. As for what is assented to by way of an incorrect admission, it is to admit something as something else due to similarity and sharing in wording or meaning, as will be known later. These are the 'sophistical premises' (mushabbahat), like someone saying: 'Every eye is seeing.' This is admitted to him insofar as one understands one of the meanings of the homonym, and he takes its substitute so that another thinks it is the intended meaning, or intends to deceive so that he falls into thinking himself or making another think that the dinar is seeing. Similarly, someone who admits that every intoxicant is wine, and takes as its substitute what is potentially intoxicating. These are the sophistical premises. As for the 'conjectural premises' (maznoonaat), they are those about which one has an opinion without a firm belief. That either is due to their resemblance to common opinions, so they become common in the first view without criticism. When scrutinized, it becomes known they are not common, like their saying: 'Support your brother whether he is the oppressor or the oppressed.' For this is opined as soon as it is heard, but when scrutinized, the common opinion is that it is not permissible to support the oppressor, whether he is a brother or a son, yet at the moment he does his deed until he scrutinizes. Or an opinion occurs on the basis of sudden acceptance, or an opinion occurs from other directions, none of which are that they are common opinions, like someone who sees and thinks it is a striking thing. These conjectural premises only occur in syllogisms insofar as there is a belief in them, not insofar as their opposite stirs in the mind. Thus all the common opinions and what was mentioned with them are beneficial where they are used, because they are believed. So any craft in which it is permissible to use conjectural premises, it is permissible to use all the mentioned ones. Likewise, common opinions are only beneficial because they are believed with a belief whose opposite does not stir in the mind, so what precedes them from the necessary things, if they are believed and granted, is as beneficial as their benefit, so it is suitable to use them where it is suitable to use those. As for the imaginary necessary premises (wahmiyyat), they are more likely to be stronger than the granted premises, not in benefit but in the intensity of the soul's acceptance without being properly evaluated. Sometimes they remain common, and sometimes they become repugnant, becoming false and repugnant together. The reason for their repugnance is not what calls to instincts, morals, and interests, but what calls to reason. The principles (mabaadi') then are: [1] imaginative (mukhayyilat), [2] sensed (mahsusat), [3] experiential (mujarrabat), [4] recurrent (mutawatirat), [5] primary (awwaliyat), [6] premises innate to syllogisms (muqaddimat fitriyyat al-qiyasat), [7] imaginary (wahmiyyat), [8] absolute common opinions (mashhoorat mutlaqa), [9] praised common opinions (mashhoorat mahmuda), [10] granted (musallamat), [11] accepted (maqbulat), [12] sophistical (mushabbahat), [13] common in first view without criticism (mashhoorat fi badi' al-ra'y al-ghayr al-mu'tatib), [14] conjectural (maznoonaat). So they are fourteen types. And here is a division of principles: those which are principles from the perspective of the one who uses them suddenly, as in the mentioned principles, but rather from the perspective of the teacher. These are the things called 'posited principles' (usul mawdu'a) and 'postulates' (musadarat).
Source pages p. 372

Commentary
This passage is a detailed classification of the types of premises (mabaadi') used in logical reasoning. The author begins by discussing 'absolute common opinions' (mashhoorat mutlaqa), which are propositions accepted by the general public, like 'justice is beautiful' or 'thankfulness is obligatory.' Their truth may not be self-evident to reason but often requires proof. Some are true under subtle conditions not recognized by the masses. The author notes that falsehood can occur among them. Next, he distinguishes common opinions that depend on a specific group (e.g., a nation or trade), called 'praised common opinions' (mashhoorat mahmuda), and those based on one or few trusted individuals, called 'accepted premises' (maqbulat). Primary intellective premises (awwaliyat) are all common, but not conversely. Then he introduces 'sophistical premises' (mushabbahat), which are accepted due to ambiguity (e.g., 'every eye is seeing'—where 'eye' could mean a sensory organ or a coin's eye; concluding that a coin can see). These are used in fallacious reasoning. Next are 'conjectural premises' (maznoonaat), which are opinions without firm belief. These may resemble common opinions but fail under scrutiny, like 'support your brother whether oppressor or oppressed'—initially accepted but later rejected. They can also arise from sudden acceptance or other unclear sources. The author stresses that these only function in syllogisms insofar as they are believed. He then summarizes that all these types are useful in arguments where belief is sufficient. Finally, he enumerates the complete list of fourteen principles: imaginative, sensed, experiential, recurrent, primary, premises innate to syllogisms, imaginary, absolute common opinions, praised common opinions, granted, accepted, sophistical, common in first view without criticism, and conjectural. He adds a separate category of principles from the teacher's perspective—'posited principles' (usul mawdu'a) and 'postulates' (musadarat). This classification is fundamental for understanding the epistemic status of premises in demonstration and dialectic.

Common Opinions and Their Variants
Author distinguishes subtypes of common opinions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish absolute common opinions (mashhoorat mutlaqa) from praised common opinions (mashhoorat mahmuda) and accepted premises (maqbulat) based on their source of acceptance.

Core Idea
Not all common opinions are equal: some are universal, others depend on a community or a trusted individual.

The passage distinguishes between several types of commonly accepted premises. Understanding these distinctions is vital for evaluating the strength of arguments in dialectical contexts, where premises are not necessarily certain but accepted by a particular group.

Guided Path
1
Identify absolute common opinions
Find the definition of 'mashhoorat mutlaqa' in the passage. Note the examples: 'justice is beautiful' and 'thanking the benefactor is obligatory.' What is their source of acceptance?
The passage says they are accepted by all, but their truth may not be self-evident.
2
Identify praised common opinions
Locate 'mashhoorat mahmuda' – these depend on a specific group (e.g., a nation or a craft). How does this differ from absolute common opinions?
They are 'praised' because they are accepted within that community.
3
Identify accepted premises
Find 'maqbulat' – premises accepted based on one or two trusted individuals. Contrast this with the first two types. Why would such premises be considered weaker?
The passage ties them to trust in specific persons.
4
Apply the distinction
Consider the example from Usul al-Fiqh: 'The consensus of the companions (ijma' al-sahaba)' – which type of common opinion is this? Why?
It is a praised common opinion because it is accepted by a specific group (the Companions).
